Then the suture will bind and clean the coagulated blood that has remained in the wound. And it was necessary to tie a string below the stone so that it would not return to the bladder, and the other string from above so that if the thread is undone after the stone comes out, the skin will return to its place and cover the wound.
So, if you tied the upper thread, you should lift the skin up so that it will return and cover the wound when you are done, as we said.
The 61st Chapter.
For Removing Stones in Women.
Stones are rarely formed in women. However, if one of them presents with a stone, it is difficult to treat and refused by many reasons. One reason is that the woman may be a virgin. The second reason is that you will not find a woman who would allow herself to be examined by a doctor.
If she is chaste or a woman related by close family ties, the third reason is that you will not find a woman who excels in this art, especially operating with one's hand. The fourth reason is that the place where the incision should be made on the stone in women is far from the place of the stone and so a deep incision is required.
And in that case, there is danger. If necessity demands it, you should appoint a skilled female surgeon, which is rarely found. If she is not available, then ask for an honest and chaste male physician, or prepare a midwife who is skilled in the matter of women, or even someone who has some knowledge in this art.
Prepare and command her to make all the investigations that you have commanded her to do regarding the stones first. And look if the woman is a virgin, then insert your finger into her vagina and investigate the stone. If you find it and press it under your finger.
Then command her to incise it, and if she is not a virgin and was married, then command the midwife to insert her finger into the woman's vagina and investigate the stone after placing her left hand on the bladder and squeezing it well.
If you find it, then you should insert it into the mouth of the bladder downwards to its capacity until it reaches the root of the thigh and then incise it when the opening is at half way between the vulva and the root of the thigh from whatever side she feels the stone in that direction.

Among them are small and large, smooth and rough, long and curved, branched and others. Know their types so you can infer what you want. If bleeding overwhelms you, leave a small amount of moistened powder in the place for a moment until the bleeding stops then return to your work.
Until the stone comes out, and know that you can use the devices I mentioned to extract stones from men to help you with your work. If bleeding overwhelms you and you understand that it is from an artery that has been cut off, then apply styptic on the place.

Chapter Fifty-seven.

And that is by the intestines coming out and its contents going out from the omentum, and sometimes the cecum comes out with it, so this colic is called intestinal and cecal and it can be accompanied by gas. And sometimes the appendix gets stuck in the intestines and dies with it.

The 67th Chapter.
On the relaxation of the skin of the testicles.

Chapter Seventy-eight.
On castration.
Castration is forbidden in our law.
Therefore, I should not have mentioned it in this book of mine; but I did so for two reasons. The first is that the physician may be asked about it and he will know its treatment if it happens to someone. The second reason is that we often need to castrate some animals for our benefit.
Like camels, donkeys and cats, and similar beasts of animals; so I say that castration can be done in two ways: either by crushing or by cutting and slitting. And that which is done by crushing: its method is that we sit the animal in hot water until the female parts relax and come down.
Then crush it with your hand until it loosens and does not become evident at the touch. And as for castration by cutting and slitting, it is necessary to hold the animal and squeeze the scrotum with the left hand, then tie back the testicles and cut on each testicle a single cut.
Until the testicles protrude, then cut them after they have become flayed and do not leave on them anything except the thin membrane that is on the vessels. And this kind of castration is better than the one by crushing.
Because crushing may have left some of the female parts behind, and then the animal would be eager for intercourse. Then treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Seventy.
On the treatment of hermaphrodites.
The hermaphrodite in men is of two kinds: one of them appears behind the pubic area or on the skin of the scrotum between the female parts, in shape like a woman's vulva with hair; and urine may flow from that which is in the skin of the scrotum.
As for women, there is a single kind which sits atop the vulva, smaller and projecting outwards like the man's penis, while the other two are like those of females. The treatment of the three kinds.
The two kinds of men and the single kind of woman should have that excessive flesh cut away until its effect is gone, then treated with the treatment of all wounds so it heals. As for the second kind of man whose urine comes out through the skin of the testicles, there is no operation or cure for this at all.
Chapter Seventy-One.
On cutting the clitoris and flesh that comes from the vulvas of women.
The clitoris may perhaps be larger than its natural size to the point that it becomes ugly and unattractive in appearance. It may even grow in some women to the extent that it spreads like a man's penis and is suitable for intercourse.
You should grasp the base of the clitoris with your hand or forceps and cut it off, without taking a long time to do so, especially when cutting deeply lest bleeding ensues.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds until it heals, and as for the growing flesh, it is a piece of flesh that grows inside the mouth of the womb to the point where it fills it.
And perhaps it will grow outside like an excrescence. Therefore, some of the ancients call it the diseased clitoris, so you should cut it off like the clitoris, either way and treat it until it heals.
Chapter Seventy-Two.
On the treatment of atresia.
Atresia is when the vulva of a woman is not pierced or has a small opening. It can be either natural, born with it, or acquired, resulting from an advanced condition, which may be due to excessive growing flesh.
A thin or thick membrane, situated either in the depth of the uterus, its walls, its upper or lower part. It prevents coitus, bandaging and childbirth, perhaps menstruation, and its presence can be determined by inspection from outside.
If the membrane is obvious near the cervix, and it is not visible, then she probes it with her fingers or a rod. If the membrane is from a thin membrane and is close to the cervix, she begins to break it, which means placing on the cervix something like a ring, then place both your thumbs together.
The woman lies on her back with her legs apart, then she thrusts the cervix with force until the thin membrane is broken and the membrane opens. Then she takes wool and dips it in oil and places it on the place and tightens the woman every day so that the place does not stick together again.
If the membrane is thick and dense, it should be cut with a broad lancet similar to an onion skin. If the membrane is from prolapsed flesh, she breaks its edges and cuts it; let there be with you medicines that stop bleeding without causing pain, such as alum, myrrh, and saffron mixed with egg whites.
Then she uses a wide tube of lead so that the wound does not close quickly. She holds it for some days and then uses threads from dry flax, then treats with other remedies to prevent adhesion until it heals. And in the uterus there may be another growth of flesh which should be cut in this manner.
Unless it is a cancerous ulcer, for a cancerous tumour in the uterus should not be exposed to iron at all.

Chapter Seventy-five.
Teaching the midwife how to treat living foetuses if they come out deformed.
The midwife should first know the natural shape of childbirth. Its signs are if you see the woman move downwards and stretch to breathe and she is in labour pains.
And if the fetus comes out quickly, know that this childbirth is natural and its exit will be on the head of the fetus with the placenta attached to it or suspended from its scrotum. If you see these signs, then compress her belly so that the fetus comes out quickly.
And if it exits on its head, the placenta will come out with it and be cleaned from that filth in a good cleaning. As for all other childbirths, they are not natural but bad because the fetus may exit on its feet or its hands before its head and feet.
And one of its hands or one of its feet may come out first, or its head with one hand, or it may be folded and perhaps turned over on its back and so forth from the bad forms. The midwife should be intelligent and gentle about all these things and beware of mistakes and errors.
And I am describing each of them and how to trick it, so you can rely on that and rely on it. If the fetus comes out on its head, coming out naturally, and it becomes stronger in the woman, causing her difficulty in delivery, and I have seen her strength weaken, then I sit her down on a chair and order the women to tie her down.
And I bind her loins with a bandage that has been cooked and moistened cloths, then the midwife inserts between her fingers a small instrument to tear the membranes or tears them with her fingernail until what is inside them, moisture flows out and compresses the woman's belly until the fetus comes down.
If it does not come down, then the woman should be injected with saliva of the bandage along with greasing the vagina, then she should order her to undress and make her sniff incense and grasp her mouth and nose, for the fetus comes out from its arm, if they have exited first, it is necessary that they are returned gently little by little.
If they do not come out to enter, then place the woman on a stool and raise her legs up high, then shake the stool on the ground and the woman grasp a support so she does not fall when shaken. If the hands have not entered and the fetus has died, take them out and attract the rest of the fetus.
Or tie his hands with a cloth and pull them out gently, for he comes out. The fetus coming out on its legs, if the fetus comes out on its legs it is necessary that you push them up then turn the fetus little by little until you straighten him then take one of his legs and extend it little by little.
If they come out to the thighs, then order her to undress and make her sniff incense, for he comes out. If he does not come out as we have described and otherwise, turn the fetus little by little until it becomes in its natural shape, for he comes out at that time smoothly, if he resists everything we have described.
Take from the saliva of a swallow and the saliva of a hoopoe and ointment of shoulder fat mixed with gum, and strike all together in a mortar well-beaten. Then pour it into the vagina and below her belly, then sit her in lukewarm water until the decoction reaches the basin.
If you see that it has been obstructed below her, then make for her a poultice of myrrh and let her wear it. If you hold the poultice for an hour, then sit her on a chair, blow into the place below her belly and close her mouth and nose, and press down there lightly; indeed, the foetus will come out from its birthplace, coming out with its legs and hands.
If it comes out in this condition, then take hold of its hands little by little. Then the woman will lie on her back and extend her legs downwards while she is lying on a bed, taking hold of her hands. Then press gently above her belly, gently, gently. Then take out what is under her feet from the pillows so that it may be raised downwards. If the foetus does not come out, then take both of her legs and shake them violently, then press gently above the basin until the foetus ascends upwards; then let the woman insert her hand and smooth out the foetus little by little.
And order the woman to undress until the foetus comes out. The foetus will come out with its arm folded and bent, attached to one of her hands. Take hold of her hand then. Then place her on her bed and shake her legs violently, then use the decoction of hooves which I have mentioned. If it takes hold, then sit her on a chair and deal with bringing back the hand and smoothing out the foetus to its natural shape. Then make the woman blow and order her to undress until the foetus comes out.
The fetus comes out on its mother's back with its arms extended and face upwards. The midwife should take hold of the fetus's hand then turn it little by little, handling it gently until it settles down. Then she should carry it in the way we have described, using a mixture of fat and olive oil.
Then the midwife should move it a little, and when she has done so, she should have her sit on a chair and tell her to lean towards the right side. She should then strip her, and make her sneeze with the onion, as this will come out, the baby standing upright on its side.
If it comes down into the uterus mouth in this form with the placenta, you should tear the placenta with your nails until it becomes dry. If it has stuck to the face or neck of the baby, then cut it off from the placenta so that it does not cause suffocation and death. Then push the baby inside until it is as it should be.
And tell the woman to sneeze again, and it will come out easily. The birth of twins or many afterbirths, know that twins are often born and sometimes three or four are born and they live, except for this being rare. And as for five, it is a thing outside of nature, which do not survive at all.

The eighty-first chapter.
Regarding piles that bleed and their cutting, and the treatment of anal fissures.
The piles are of two kinds, either they occur inside the anus and resemble fresh red blisters or like grapes and there are small and large ones, and blood always flows from them.
And they are either one or many, and they occur outside the anus, especially at its edges; but those that occur outside the anus are in most cases pale and there is a constant flow of yellowish water or little bleeding, and they have the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which occurs inside the anus is to order the patient to be constipated and purged until the anus comes out and the piles become visible, then rush quickly and grasp them with forceps or hold them with your fingernails and then cut them at their roots; if the forceps do not stick in them because of their moisture and laxness.

Then treat them with the rest of the treatment until they are healed, if the anus does not come out quickly then have the patient inject a little hot water into it to wash away what is inside and aid its exit when the patient defecates, so take out the piles that have come out easily.
And this is to take hold of it with your fingernails or grasp it with forceps and cut it, then treat it as we mentioned until it heals, and if the patient dislikes cutting with iron it is necessary that you use its string in this manner: take a loose thread and insert it into a needle.
Then, the excrescence is drawn upwards and pushed in with a needle from the other side. The ends of the thread are twisted below the needle as it is bent, and the excrescence is tightly tied. Then, the thread is knotted and the needle withdrawn. This is done for all excrescences except one which is left untouched.
Then, let the excess blood ooze out of it. Then, put on the bed a cloth soaked in rose oil or safflower oil and place it on the bed and command the ulcer to be quiet. Then, leave it until it falls off. If they have fallen off, treat them with ointments and all other treatments until they heal.
And as for the treatment of fissures, many times they are found on the walls of the anus and the natural temperament is obstructed. Then, treat it until it heals as it should. If it does not heal, repeat the procedure with a tougher scraping than the first one until it is ulcerated and peels off. Then, treat it and it will heal.
The Second Chapter.
In the treatment of inverted and non-inverted splinters and dry ulcers and abscesses.
The splinter is a round knot on the surface of the body resembling the head of the splinter, occurring in all parts of the body and especially in the lower legs and fingers, and it presents with pain when walking.
It is necessary to incise the area around the splinter and grasp it with a probe or a forceps and remove it from its root, then treat it. And if you wish, clean it as mentioned in its chapter. As for dry ulcers, one of them is a small, rough, flat, rounded growth above the surface of the body.
Often it appears in the hands of children and its treatment is to draw out the boil and cut it or ligate it with a silk thread or hair until it falls and if you want to cauterize it as mentioned above with fire or with a corrosive drug.
And boils that are inflamed and un-inflamed can be removed with the device which I have mentioned in the treatment of the flea. As for the flea, it is also a small, thick, moist swelling on the surface of the body that goes deep into it; if it gets cold, one feels something similar to the stinging of the flea.
And it will be all over the body, and most of it is in the hands. Its treatment is to take a tube from an ostrich feather or a strong eagle feather and make that tube around the flea until a circle forms around it then turn your hand with the tube until it cuts what is around the flea.
And it will be in the depth of the flesh and the flea will come out from its root. And if you wanted to do that with a copper or iron tube on this form:
The top of the tube is closed and opened to the skin so that the fingers can easily grasp it and open it, and if you want to cauterize the flea as mentioned above in the chapter on cauterizing boils.

The Fourth and Eighth Chapter.
In the treatment of wounds.
I have mentioned in the classification of what resembles cauterization, that I am describing its treatment by hand and with medicine to perfection.  I say that wounds vary according to what the injury is in and according to where the wound occurs.
And the things with which wounds occur are numerous, such as a stone cut or sword cuts or knife cuts or arrow or spear thrusts and similar things.
And as for wounds according to the parts of the body, such as a wound on the head or neck or chest or belly or liver and similar organs.
And I am describing the treatment of some wounds in order to make them a measure and a rule for all other wounds, and I begin with the simple head wounds, particularly because the compound ones will be mentioned first in chapter three of this book.
And I say, if a simple wound occurs in the head and there is no bone fracture, look; if it was caused by a stone or the like and had only torn the skin and the wound was large and I feared an abscess would occur, then hasten to apply the cautery on the spot. And do not delay in doing so, and let him bleed as much blood from the wound as his strength allows, unless a preventer of bleeding prevents it, especially if the blood coming out of the wound is slight; let the bleeding be from the side opposite to the injury.
And warn the patient against overfilling, drinking and heavy foods; if a preventer of bleeding prevents him from bleeding, then let him reduce his food and not get close to meats or broths. If an abscess hot fever occurs on it, then place on the wound cotton soaked in rose oil alone or with the drink that has myrrh.
If the inflamed swelling is cured, place it on the wound if it is moist with blood and not changed by air. And if it has been changed by air, place some ointments on it.
Until the discharge is abundant, then treat it until it clears. If the wound is large and was caused by a sword or similar cut and its edges have not joined together with the flesh, sew it up as I described in sewing belly wounds.
If the skin has exposed the bone and adhered to it without being firmly attached except for a small part, cut him off then treat the wound with medicines that will form in it a hard flesh instead of the skin. And order the patient to eat nourishments such as heads and trotters and similar things.
If there is putrefaction in the skin and it does not adhere to the bone, cut off the rotten part completely and dress with it then treat the wound. If there is an artery or vein that is bleeding from it and the blood has not stopped with medicines, search for the artery.
If it becomes inflamed, do not cut it. If inflammation occurs, cut it with a probe and bandage it. And if necessity demands it, and what we have mentioned does not benefit, apply to the wound until the bleeding stops. And if these wounds are small and simple, they can be treated by applying this powder on them as long as there is blood in them.
Before the air changes it, the recipe of this powder is: take a part from alum, two parts from bitumen and three parts from either burnt or unburnt plaster. Crush them all, sift and mix with the wound, compress it well so that a good coagulum forms on it.
It is constricted by the blood, then it is compressed from above with a suture and left as long as it does not become inflamed. For it will not come off until it heals. And sometimes the plaster alone can do this if alum and bitumen are not available to you.
He can also do this act with a bandage in many large wounds, if he has mastered its placement and tightening, and the wound is fresh with blood, unless it has been altered by air, but especially if it is in the summer season.
Let him not think that what we have mentioned about placing a poultice on this plaster has the meaning of applying some made-up ointments or a pudding made from barley flour with water and honey until it swells, then treating it with other treatments until it heals.
But if the fracture occurred with the wound in a bone and it was slight, he should grasp it with a splint. I have mentioned the treatment of fractures of the skull in what follows about neck surgery; if the wound occurred in the neck, there is no difference in the treatment between this and a simple head wound.
And if a vertebra or artery has been cut, then there is no remedy for it except that he should avoid its treatment with what would tightly constrict him like pincers and pliers and similar things because they harm the nerve and stretch it.
And nothing cold should be put on the wound at all because of the nature of the nerve being cold and its connection to the brain which is the most noble of organs, and what he treats it with from liquid medicines like washing a turpentine with sweet water several times mixed with oil or rose and date syrup.
If he also washes it with sweet water and all the metallic stones in this nature and wet ointments, and if the wound is large, then use suturing or compress his lips with a pad then treat him until he heals.
If the wound has a depth and there is a collection in its bottom, he should tie it in the lowest place of it. And if an artery was cut off in the wound and he noticed his blood flowing out, then he should ligate it or bandage it or cauterize it if necessity demands that.
If the wound had cut some or all of the ligaments of the throat and the arteries were left intact, then join together the edges of the skin with stitching on the handle of the gullet. Do not touch the gullet at all but rather sully it and restore it to its natural shape, then tighten it strongly and leave it for a few days.
Then treat it with ointments that contain astringent and drying until it heals. If the wound had affected the bone, then scrape it. If there are fragments of bone in it, then extract them all or as much as possible, and if you cannot do so, leave it until the wound heals, dries out, and becomes easy to remove.
If the wound is moist, then apply to it the powder that we have described. And if a piece of bone becomes encrusted in the wound and becomes hard as stone, then treat it with what will be mentioned later in its chapter on thoracic surgery and what lies between the shoulders.
If it was a stab from an arrow or sword and you saw pus coming out of it, then look. If the breath escaped from it when the patient breathed in, then know that it is a fighting wound. And if it did not have pus and was moist with blood, then do not put powder in it at first sight and do not tighten it lest the blood gets stuck in its depths.
And he should be distracted from the heart and killed the patient, but put in it a sticky ointment and leave it to dry out during the day twice. And if you do not have an ointment, then put in the mouth of the wound a thick cotton so as to absorb what comes out of it from moisture, and make the patient sleep on the wound.
As the moisture accumulates in it. And if three or more days had passed since the wound and there was no disturbance, nor a rough pulse, nor any tightness of the breath and you saw all his conditions were good, then know that the wound is safe, treat it with sutures and the rest of the treatment until it heals.
If it is difficult to see and you keep inserting a probe, know that it has become necrotic from its base. The healer should evacuate what accumulates in the wound, if it was for chest or back wounds he should perform stitching as previously mentioned.
If it is large or in the joints, if small, if it has affected the bone and cut into fragments then investigate the wound and hurry to remove those fragments if they are suppurating, if they are not suppurating then leave them.
If the wound suppurates, it becomes easy to remove them, and as for other wounds that occur in all parts of the body, their treatment is ruled by what we mentioned.
The Twenty-Fifth Chapter.
In the wound of the abdomen and the evacuation of feces and its stitching.
The tear which is exposed to the abdomen can be large or small or medium, and if it is large feces or several intestines may come out so its insertion and stitching will be harder.
And the small tear also has another difficulty which makes it necessary to be harder to return, and the intestines if you do not hurry to put them back in their place from its hour they will swell and thicken so that it is difficult to insert them, therefore the best wound was the medium-sized one because it does not make returning the feces as difficult as in these two types.
Know that stitching the abdomen is on four faces, the two common stitches are suitable for stitching the abdomen and stitching other wounds of the body, and the other two special stitches cover all wounds except that they are more specific to stitching the abdomen.
And I am describing it to you one by one with explanation, so I say that if the wound is small and something from the intestines has come out and it is difficult to return it then this is for one of two reasons either because of the smallness of the tear as we said or because the intestine was inflated in contact with the cold air.

And also, if the wound is in one side of the abdomen, then make it so that the side with the incision is always higher than the other side; and thus it should be used for large wounds and medium-sized ones.

This is the description of a general suture. She takes a needle or several needles according to the size of the wound, then she leaves from the edge of the incision a space equal to the thickness of a nail and she sews a single thread without passing a thread in both edges of the skin. Along the edges of the peritoneum, which is inside from under the skin until she reaches them from that side. And she has joined the two layers of the skin and the two layers of the peritoneum, so they became four folds, then she pulls with a double thread around the needle times from both sides altogether until the lips of the wound meet firmly together.
Then she leaves also the thickness of a finger, and she sews another needle, then she entwines it with the thread as she did with the first needle, and so she continues to do that with as many needles as she needs until she has completed closing up all of the wound, and let the needles be distributed between the thick and thin parts.
Because the fine needles quickly cut through the flesh and also it is difficult to enter them into the skin, therefore they should be of medium thickness in both fineness and thickness. And it is not advisable to insert the needle into the edge of the skin very close together.
So that the flesh will not quickly tear and the wound will not open up before being closed. And it should also not be distant from the sewing, so that the wound will refuse to close. Then she cuts off the ends of the needles, so that they do not harm the patient when he sleeps and make a swelling on his body from the incision from every side, holding with linen threads the ends of the needles.
And she leaves them until she learns that the wound has closed, and this type of stitching with needles is more suitable for small wounds because it may suffice to sew them with a needle or two or something like that.
As for the technique of stitching in the second year, it is that you gather by suturing the four edges, namely the skin and the peritoneum at once with a needle containing a moderately twisted thread in fineness and thickness. And when these four edges have been carried out with the needle, take back the needle from the side from which it began itself.
So let the thread be tied from above the wound so that the suturing is according to the suturing of a needle in which goods are sewn and make between each suture and suture after thickening the little finger, and this suture is known by everyone.

Chapter Seventy-Eight.
For cutting off limbs and spreading bones.
The limbs may putrefy either from an external cause or an internal one, if you have treated the corruption with a remedy and it did not succeed and you see the corruption is spreading in the member without anything holding it back.
You should then cut off that member as far as the corruption has spread so that the patient will be saved from death or a great affliction which is greater than losing the member, and a sign of its occurrence is when the member becomes swollen until you think that fire has burned it.
Or it may putrefy after swelling until the corruption spreads to what follows that member and takes hold of a part of the body, then cut it off. Similarly if the cause of the corruption is due to being bitten by some vermin such as scorpions or snakes or rodents etc.
If the corruption or bite is in the tip of the finger, cut off the finger and do not delay for the corruption to spread to the hand. Likewise if it has taken hold of the hand, cut it off from the wrist joint and do not delay for the corruption to spread until it takes hold of the arm's joint.
And likewise if it has taken hold of the arm, cut off the arm at the shoulder joint in the same joint. If the corruption spreads and you see that it is approaching towards the armpit, do not cut the armpit at all because in that case the patient will die, and instead use some other treatment as far as possible.
Also do the same with man, if he took a corruption in the finger cut the finger at one of the joints, and if he took to comb his hair cut the leg completely because if it rose up to the knee cut the lower part of the thigh at the joint of the knee.
And if the corruption has reached above the knee then there is no remedy except to leave it and bind the limb up until death, A method of cutting off or spreading an organ that you pull a strip in the lower part of the place which you want to cut and tie another strip above the place.

Lest the knife touch the correct place and expose it to the invalid's excessive pain and hot swelling, if bleeding occurs during your work then stop the area quickly or put some sharp coagulants on it then return to your treatment until you finish.
Then bind the wounded member with a strap suitable for it and treat it until it heals, And I inform you of an example that occurred to a man in his leg this ulcer as I have described to you, and it happened in his leg a blackness with a burning heat like fire.

Chapter Eighty-Eight.
In the treatment of boils and how to lance them with medicine.
If a boil occurs in some of the fleshly organs and its duration is prolonged until it gathers pus then bursts or splits and all that was inside the pus comes out, leaving the place empty like an empty vessel.
And the skin over it, like a cloth, has become loose and is not very corrupted that it affects bone or nerve or ligament. From here it deserves to be called an abscess rather than boils except if its duration is prolonged until the corruption affects something of these organs.
So when that happens it is called a carbuncle or a boil. And its treatment is to cut out all of that skin, as it peels off and becomes like cloth, especially if it has become loose and does not stick to the place because of corruption.
And if I hoped that the skin would stick and it was not so corrupted to that extent, and there is a thick layer of flesh under the skin, then treat him with an injection. And look at the abscess, if it is large and the pus that flows from it has a very foul smell, inject medicine into it.
Which is a mixture of vinegar, oil, honey, and ginger, its parts are equal whether they are gathered in a vessel and cooked on the fire until the medicine thickens and comes to be like thick honey. Then take what you need from it and mix it with water and honey, inject it into the abscess and close his mouth and leave the medicine in him for as long as two hours.
Then take him to a hot bath for several days until the wound is clean and the foul odor has gone. If this remedy is not available, he can be washed with ash water; take the ash of grapevine wood or walnut wood, mix it with water.
Then wipe him clean and wash him with it until you know that the wound has been thoroughly cleansed. If there is no possibility of using this medicine or ash water, then he should be washed with honey and water mixed together with some crushed ginger.
Or else instill honey and the drink into him, mixed together, because it is the property of honey to clean and wash, and the drink to clot the wound, especially if the drink contains vinegar and glue. If you have done that several times and known that his corruption has left him, then instill into him what sprouts in the flesh.
Like if you take the empty ointment tube and fill it with rosewater, coagulant drink or some of the other medicines and ointments whose properties we have mentioned in the chapter on ointments. If the mouth of the wound is narrow so that the instillation tube cannot fit into it.
Then sew it with a little iron or place some sodden lint in the Egyptian ointment or the srocket ointment until it expands. Similarly, if his mouth is also wide, then bring together his lips with sewing and leave on him just as much as the instillation tube can enter into without excess.
And also if his mouth, from which the pus flows out, is raised upwards then cut it in a lower place so that the pus can flow out downwards because if the pus becomes congealed at the bottom of the wound, the flesh will not sprout into it.
And if you are unable to cut the wound downwards as you want, then turn the part upwards so that the pus can flow out easily according to what is prepared for you and make sure that nothing from the pus gets congealed in it whatever.
As for the medicinal plaster which is put on the place of pain from outside and how it should be applied, take a portion of one of the compounded plasters which you want to use and spread it over a linen cloth so that the entire place is covered by it. So understand that the abscess has stuck or approached sticking, then drain it on the fourth day or in the third. Then the healing is near because among people some have delayed flesh growth in their wounds and some are contrary to that. If something pus comes out from the place of pain after many days without being thick, know that the abscess has not healed if the abscess heals slowly.
And the treatment took a long time, so add more linseed and let it be in consistency wet and in strength dry like the lukewarm plaster if it had been added to it. Among the most effective treatments is to take the lukewarm plaster.

The 88th chapter.
In the treatment of gangrene and infected wounds, cutting off extra fingers and splitting joined fingers.
The gangrene is a large mass of flesh that grows under the toe or finger nail, and perhaps in other fingers. If its condition is neglected and its treatment delayed it becomes swollen and hot.
or it becomes rotten and putrid until it eats away the base of the nail, and perhaps it corrupts all of it, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone so that it has a bad smell and the tip of the finger becomes swollen and its color is like inflammation. If you treat it with what we mentioned in the chapter and it does not recover from your treatment.
So it is necessary to cut with iron all the growth that grows from the nail, then cauterize the wound afterwards because cautery is very beneficial in this case. And if the bone was sound and the nail was also sound.
The corner containing the nail had clotted the flesh and made it inflamed. It is necessary to place a thin probe under the angle of the nail that has clotted the flesh, lift it up, and cut that flesh gently.
And she puts on the remaining flesh from the burning effective remedies until it all goes away, then treats it with ointments until it clears up. But if corruption has affected the bone, it is necessary to cut that bone and remove it; for it will not clear up as long as there is a corrupt bone in it.

Then cut the nail with a sharp lancet, a crooked incision from top to bottom, and be careful that you do not reach the flesh under the nail because by doing so you will cause severe pain in the abscess and lead to an overgrowth of excessive flesh in the place, then treat the place with what alleviates the pains.
And as for the supernumerary finger which is generated in some hands of people, it may be all flesh, or in some of it bones, or in some a nail, and there may be an overgrowth of some of it at the base of one of the joints of the fingers.
Or its overgrowth may be at some of the joints of the fingers, and that which grows in some of the joints of the fingers does not move, and that which grows at the joint of the finger perhaps moves. What is all flesh from it cut off easily; cut it off at its base with a broad lancet.
And as for that which grows at the joint of the finger, its treatment is difficult and you should not cut it off. The Ninetieth Chapter.
For cutting abscesses.
The dawali are twisted and tortuous veins filled with thick black blood which occur in most parts of the body and most often happen in the legs, especially in those who carry heavy burdens or loads, so it is necessary first to use a rub from the black powder several times vigorously.
Then apply to the owner of the dawali a caustic, and as for treating it with iron, there are two ways: one that cuts and comes out the black blood, and the other lets the vein discharge its contents completely. If cutting is done, then the leg should be first wrapped in hot water.
Until the thick black blood is dissolved, then bind the leg of the patient from above his knee to below his ankle with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one place or two places or three wide incisions.
Then let the black blood be discharged with your hand from below the leg to above and from above to below until it comes out of the quantity which you see sufficient or as much as the patient can tolerate, then bind him and order him to avoid foods that produce black bile, and repeat evacuation and lancing.
When the blood vessels are full and this harms the patient, as for his purging, it will be in this manner: the leg of the patient is to be shaved if there is much hair on it then he enters the bath and drenches his leg with hot water until it reddeness and break out into sweat or a strong sweating occurs.
If the bath does not warm up the member, then the skin is cut along the length of the sweat, either at its end near the knee or lower down at the ankle, then the skin is opened with a surgical instrument and the sweat is scraped away from all sides until it becomes apparent by touch.
And when it appears, it is seen as a red, bloody thing. If he finishes with the skin, it is also seen like a string, then a probe is inserted under it until if it rises and comes out of the skin it is tied back with a smooth, blind needle, then another incision is made in an incision-like manner about that cut to the extent of three fingers.
Then the skin is scraped away from over the sweat until it appears, then he raises it up with a probe as was done and ties it back with another needle as was done first, then another incision or many incisions are made if that is needed.
Then he purges it and cuts it off at the end of the incision near the ankle, then he pulls it and purges it until it comes out from the second incision, then he pulls it to the incision above it and does that until he pulls all of it out from the third incision above all the incisions, so when all of it is come out, he cuts it off.
If it does not come out with pulling and purging, then enter a strong double thread needle and tie it, then pull it and insert a probe under it and close your hand to all sides until it comes out and take care that it is not cut off because if it is cut off it will be very hard for you to purge it.
He enters into the wound of the patient a noxious thing, so if he has drained it all, place on the site of the incisions wool soaked in vinegar or rose oil or olive oil and treat him until he recovers. If the vein is varicose with folds and twists twisted to one side.

The Ninetieth Chapter.
In draining the vein which is not inflamed.
This vein is formed in the legs in hot countries like Hijaz and the lands of the Arabs, and in hot regions with little humidity. And it may be formed in other places of the body besides the legs and its formation occurs due to a putridness that takes place under the skin.
As it occurs in the living bodies, and the leech, and the flea, and the boil that forms between the skin and the flesh. And the sign of the beginning of this vein's formation is that there is a severe inflammation in the leg, then the area becomes inflamed, then the vein begins to come out from the site of that inflammation.
As if it were the root of a plant or an animal, so if its tip appears, you should wrap around it a small piece of lead, weighing from one to two drachmas, and bind it and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever something comes out of him to the outside, wrap it in lead and tie it. If it has been a long time, cut some of it and wind up the rest. Do not cut it from its origin before everything is out of it, for if you do, it will shrink and get into the flesh, causing an abscess and bad sore.
For this reason, it should be treated and pulled on a little at a time until everything comes out of the body. And in some people, what comes out of this abscess can be five finger's breadth long and ten.
And I have been told that it came out for a man twenty finger's breadth long. If one is cut off from you while treating him, insert a rod into the hole and pull with all your strength until everything comes out of it from matter. And try to clean the place with medicine for a few days.
Then treat it with the treatment of tumors. The Ninetieth Chapter.
Regarding the incision for a boil that occurs under the navel and is called "the scourge of cattle".
This disease is called the scourge of cattle in some of our countries because it is often exposed to cattle, and it is a small worm that is born between the skin and flesh and spreads throughout the body, rising and falling.
It is perceived by touch when it moves from one part to another until it bursts where ever it burst in the skin and comes out, and its formation from the corruption of some organs, as well as being exposed to maggots and grubs and locusts in the belly.
And it is expected from its harm that if it moves in the body and rises to the head and reaches the eye, it may open there and come out, ruining the eye. And often this occurs. If you want to treat it and remove it, then it can only be done when it has moved and appeared by touch.
You should tighten what is above and below it with a good suture, then cut on it and take it out. If it sinks into the flesh without finding it, carry fire to the spot until it burns it. The most to be expected of it is to ruin the eye as we said.
If you see it has become in the head near the eye, then tie under it to the forehead with a good suture, then cut on it and take it out. It is necessary for the surgeon to purify his body with easy remedies for the foul humors that are corrupted and to be cautious from nourishing foods that generate phlegm.
The Third and Ninetieth Chapter.
In the cut for the illness known as cataract.
This illness, which is known in our land as naffer, is a pain that appears in some organs then moves from organ to organ and I have seen it as I described to you.
I was called to an elderly woman in some villages, I uncovered her arm and made a slight blowing into the vein of the arm's tendon. When an hour had passed I saw that the swelling began to creep upwards like a worm with the bandage advancing towards her shoulder as fast as possible, like mercury.
If it moved from place to place, the pain would leave that place and settle in the shoulder, then I sat for an hour, it spread throughout the body until it reached the other arm, then she told me that her whole body was turning over as I had seen.
I was amazed at its speed in moving from organ to organ and I had not seen this illness with my own eyes like that before, except that I saw a group of people finding the pain moving from organ to organ but I did not see it with my own eyes as I have seen it in this woman.
And I did not consider that to be the case except that the woman was a Bedouin, lean body, exposed skin, so that's how the sense showed the moving air and it had to appear on this scale in people of prosperity, with soft bodies, hidden skin.
If you wish to treat him and he feels pain, then if the swelling appears as we have mentioned, bind over it with a roller (or a tightly rolled cloth) both above and below, and cut into it until the accumulated pus comes out and the place is evacuated.
If you do not see it with your eyes, then stimulate him by a dusting of the body and what drives out the wind and breaks up the accumulation, such as mustard plaster and poultice made from sacking and the like of remedies.
The Ninetieth Chapter (or Section).
For removing arrows.
The arrows may differ in accordance with their kinds and in accordance with the places where they are found in the body. As for their difference according to their kinds, there are large ones and small ones, some made of transparent glass and others made of solid glass, and those that have three edges and four edges.
And there are those with barbs and those with fragments. And as for the ones that occur according to the organs in which they reside, they are of two kinds: either the organs are major flat organs such as the brain, heart, liver, lungs, kidneys, intestines, bladder and like them. When an arrow falls in one of these organs and the signs of death which I have described to you become apparent, then you should avoid removing that arrow from it, because death will befall its owner most of the time. And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the organ, then take it out and treat the wound. And one of the signs of the brain being hit by an arrow is that the bone has been pierced and a wound was inflicted on the omentum which is above the brain; from this there results severe headache, fever, and dizziness.
And when these bad signs do not appear to you and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the organ, then take it out and treat the wound. And one of the signs of the brain being hit by an arrow is that the bone has been pierced and a wound was inflicted on the omentum which is above the brain; from this there results severe headache, fever, and dizziness. And redness in the eyes, and redness of the tongue, and spasms and confusion of mind, and spitting once. And perhaps blood comes out from the nostrils or the ears. And perhaps speech is lost and sound is gone, and fluid comes out from the place of the wound white and like gruel, and it flows like serum of flesh.
If these signs appear to you, refrain from treating the patient and extracting the arrow if it has not come out; as for the symptoms of the arrow, if the heart is positioned close to the left breast and you feel something solid with it as if it had been stuck in a solid thing, not an empty space.
Perhaps the arrow's movement resembles a pulse, and black blood flows from the wound; then follows a cold sweat with chills, and a delirium. Know that death is sure to come; a sign of the arrow if it has injured the lung is the emission of frothy blood from the wound.
And the veins near the neck become swollen, and the patient's complexion changes; he breathes with a high respiratory rate, and asks for cold air inhalation. If the arrow has pierced the membrane in the chest, it will be close to the lower ribs; there is a very great difficulty of breathing along with severe pain.
And he contracts and moves all the limbs; if the arrow has pierced the liver, there follows severe pain and blood comes out from the wound similar in color to the liver. If the arrow has pierced the stomach, perhaps something of undigested food comes out from the wound.
And it is clear, if the arrow has pierced the abdomen and there was a discharge from the wound of feces, pus, or an intestine that had ruptured, then there is no hope for his recovery nor to extract the arrow; if the arrow has pierced the bladder and urine comes out.
And something protrudes from it to the outside, and the pain intensifies on the patient, so know that he is doomed; but as for other organs like the face, eye, throat, shoulder, arm, lumbar region, buttocks, thigh, leg and similar organs, you have taken on most of the case.
When the arrow did not hit a vein or an artery, and was not poisoned, and I inform you of some of what I have seen regarding these arrows so that you can use it as a basis for your treatment; that a man had an arrow lodged in his nose at its base.
And I extracted it from him on the other side, under the top of his ear and he was cured and no ill occurred in his eye. And I extracted another arrow from a Jew who had shot it into the top of his eye under the lower eyelid, and the arrow had disappeared except for its small tip which stuck in the bone.
And it was a large arrow from the assembled arrows of iron, quadrangular in shape, smooth and had no point. And the Jew was cured and no evil occurred to his eye. And I extracted another arrow from a Christian's throat and the arrow was Arab.
And it was the one who had two eyes, and I cut open on him between the pectorals, and he had a arrow in his throat. So I straightened him with it until I extracted it, then the Christian was cured. And I extracted another arrow from a man to whom it had been shot into his belly, and we estimated that he would die from it.
When the period of thirty days or near it had passed, and nothing changed in his condition, I cut into the arrow and cleansed him with it and extracted it. He was cured and no evil occurred to him. And I saw a man upon whom an arrow had been shot into his back, so I sealed over the wound on him.
When seven years had passed, the arrow came out of its base and fell out. And I saw a woman upon whom an arrow had been shot into her belly, so I sealed over the wound on her, and the arrow remained and nothing changed in her condition nor did she find any harm from it in anything of her natural actions.
And I saw another man upon whom an arrow had been shot into his face, so I sealed over the wound on him, and it remained and he did not find much pain in it. And many like this one. And I extracted an arrow from a man who was a squire of the Sultan, which had been shot into the middle of his nose, having tilted to the right side a little.
The arrow had passed through completely. I was summoned to treat him after the arrow had been in place for three days. I found that the wound from the arrow was very narrow, so I probed it with a thin probe and did not feel anything. But he felt numbness and pain under his ear on the right side from the cut.
I was hesitant that this numbness might be from the tip of the arrow, so I applied a sticking plaster with strong adhesive to hold him in place and tighten it slightly in order for the area to become swollen and the sign of the arrow to appear. Then I incised (made an incision) on him but nothing happened in the area that would indicate that the arrow had reached that location.
I continued to apply the plaster on him for several days, but nothing happened. Then I sealed his wound with a medicinal dressing and he remained numb from it until a day passed when he felt something in his nose and I was informed of this. So I placed on the wound a hot and pungent medicine for several days.
Until it opened and I probed him with the medicine. Then I felt the thin tip of the arrow which stuck in the windpipe. So I continued to open up the wound with this pungent medicine until the tip of the arrow appeared clearly before us. He then recovered from it over a period of time of about four months.
Then when the wound had widened and I was able to insert forceps into it, I grasped it with them and moved it. But he did not respond by bringing it out. So I did not leave off soothing him or applying various instruments to him until one day I was able to grasp the arrowhead firmly in the forceps as depicted at the end of this chapter.
And the doctors were accustomed to saying that the flesh of his nose would not close, but it did close and the wound healed perfectly. And I am telling you how some arrows came out in such a way that this becomes an example and a proof of what I have mentioned.
Because the parts of this craft and its details cannot be grasped by description or encompassed in a book. The ingenious craftsman measures little for much, anticipates what is lacking, and deduces a new procedure from a strange case.
If one descends from this craft, I say that the arrows are extracted from the parts in which they have been lodged into two types; either by pulling them out of the place from where they entered, or from the opposite side, of the part from which they exited.
Either the arrow is protruding in a place of flesh, and it is pulled out. But if you are unable to remove it in its proper time, then you should leave it for some days until the flesh around it has softened, so that it can be easily removed and extracted. Likewise, if it has lodged in a bone.
If you are unable to remove it, leave it also for some days and repeat pulling and moving it each day; then it will come out. If after some days you are still unable to remove it, you should drill around the arrow in the same bone from all sides with a fine drill until there is room for the arrow, then pull it out and extract it.
If the arrow has lodged in the skull and is pointed in one of the cavities of the brain and some of those symptoms have appeared from the wound, then stop pulling on the arrow and leave it until matters settle down after a few days.
So occupy yourself with pulling out the arrow and removing it. And if it has penetrated deeply and you are unable to pull it, then use a drill around the arrow as I have described to you, then treat the place until it heals. But if the arrow has disappeared in some part of the body. If he lacks sensation, he is to probe him with a stylet. If he feels it, then he is to grasp it with some instruments suitable for grasping it. And if she was unable on account of the tightness of the wound and the remoteness of the arrow in the body cavity and there were no bone, nerve, or artery.
She is to split him open until the wound is widened and she can get at the arrow so that she may extract it. And if there are ears, she is to grasp them and release all the flesh within them from every side with whatever device she can use, and hold tight if she cannot relieve the flesh in a break of the ears and split them until she has relieved herself.
And if she tried to extract the arrow in any place, and was unable, then she is to use a linen cloth on her hand towards all sides until it is cleaned and smoothed out so that the tip of the arrow does not break off for her. And if she was still unable from her time.
Then leave it for some days until the flesh around it has become rotten, and then she is to continue. For it will be easy at that time. And if there is bleeding from him, then let her use what we mentioned in its treatment. And conserve your strength and do not attempt to cut a nerve or an artery or a tendon, and employ cunning.
You can extract the arrow from all sides with ease, being gentle and cautious as I have described to you. And it is advisable for you to employ a cauterizing iron in the shape that the arrow was in when it pierced him, it will be more effective.
If you are unable to do that, then use whatever shape you can. And as for the arrow which comes out from the opposite side, either it has protruded something outside or else you will find the tip of the arrow with your hand from above the skin close by and see it projecting.
Split him open, and the incision should be as long as you can insert a linen cloth into it. Then grasp it, for it will be easy for it to come out. And if it is stuck in bone, then spread your hand round until the arrow penetrates the bone and widens its space for itself, then grasp it.
If not, then leave it for some days until he forgets, and when the arrowhead is still within him, push it out with a rod. And if the arrow shaft has fallen and you wanted to use force, then insert into it the instrument that is grooved so that it goes inside the cavity of the arrow shaft, then push it out with it.
If the arrow is grooved, push it out with a tool that enters into that groove; for it makes the arrow easy to come out. And if the arrow was poisoned, it should be cut off the flesh which had the poison in it, if you can do so, then treat him as one needs in such a case.
If the arrow is in the chest or belly or bladder or side and it was close to something that can be felt with a probe and you could cut into it, then cut into it, being careful not to cut an artery or nerve, and remove it.
Then suture the wound if it needs sewing, then treat him until he recovers. The drawing of various hooks to attract arrows:.

The fifth and ninetieth chapter.
In letting out veins.
The veins which are accustomed to be let out in the body, thirty veins; from the head, sixteen veins: the two jugular veins behind the ears and known as al-husayn, the two facial veins on the temples and known as al-zahran, and the two veins in the corners of the eyes and known as an-nazir.
The erect hair on the forehead, and the hair at the tip of the nose, and those on the neck and the two hairs on the upper cheek from the mouth and the two hairs on the lower cheek. And these known as al-jahark, and the two under the tongue.
And as for the hairs that spread out on the arm and hand, they are five hairs. One of them is al-qifaal, which is from the savage side and it is known to the common people as the hair of the head. Al-akhaal is the middle one, which is composed of a branch of al-basliiq and a branch of al-qifaal and it is known to the common people as the hair of the body.
And al-basliiq which is located in the human side and it is also known as the armpit and it is known to the common people as the hair of the belly and the arm tendon, which is the one on the bony part that sticks to it and appears above the elbow in a clear form, and al-asislam.
And it is the hair that is between the kneecap and the ankle bone, having two branches. And on the leg and foot there are three hairs, one of which is under the knee from the savage side, and the second is al-saafin and its place is at the ankle from the human side, and the hair of the calf and its place is at the heel from the savage side.
And on the other leg there are three hairs like them. As for the two hairs behind the ears, their benefit is to repel chronic and acute rhinitis, sneezing and head rashes. How their remedy is as I have described.
And it is that you shave the head of the one who has them and stroke the back of his head in the place of the hairs with a rough cloth well, then he will choke the one who has them with a turban until the two hairs appear, and their places are behind the ears in the lowered parts of the head, so it will resemble them with your finger and where you felt their pulse under your finger, there apply the ink.
Then take a skinned probe, known as the nashl. Insert it under the hair on the skin until the probe reaches the bone. Then raise your hand with the hair and skin upwards, and cut the hair along with the skin into separate cuts of a length equal to two folded fingers.
Or take the amount of blood you want from it, then pull them tight with threads and leave them until they heal. As for the arteries in the temples, it is beneficial to divert them for longstanding migraines, severe headaches, and persistent redness aimed at the eyes.
And how to divert them as I have described to you, tie the patient's neck with a turban until the arteries appear clearly and their pulse is visible under your finger. Then learn them with ink, then raise the skin from above the artery with your thumb.
Then insert the nashl probe from below and raise the artery up, cut it as you did with the other arteries, and take out the amount of blood that suits your need. Then remove the tourniquet from the patient and place your finger on the artery for a moment.
Then place a piece of catgut and thread on it, tie from above tightly and leave it until it heals. As for diverting the artery of the forehead, its benefit comes after diverting the temporal arteries for longstanding facial problems such as eczema, ulcers and fine redness.
And how to divert it as I have described to you, constrict the patient's neck with a turban until the artery appears. Then take the instrument called al-fas (the saw), and this is its shape:.
Place the pointed spike that is in the head of the saw on the same artery, and strike from above with a comb or something else similar to it, and let the blood flow into the vessel that you want. Then remove the tourniquet from the patient and tie it tightly until it heals.
The incision should be made with a broad probe, except that the probe should not have a sharp end like other probes. Rather it should be slightly broad at one end and tapered towards its tip because the bone is close by; perhaps the probe will break if it is too fine.
As for the two probes which are in the conjunctival sac of the eyes, their benefit is for the diseases of the eye such as trachoma and redness and inflammation and facial disease. As for how to do it, it is that one binds the patient's head with a turban then you make the incision while he is standing on his head and the incision should be slightly tapered towards its length a little bit with a small probe which is somewhat broad.
If the place is fat, then the incision should not be made with a thin-edged probe because it might break; then you will send in what you need from blood and put on them a piece of gauze and tie them for one night then take them out. As for draining the nasal vein, it is beneficial for acute fever.
The severe headache and the diseases of the face such as the redness which appears in the nose, especially if it is chronic. And how to do it: he binds the patient's head then takes hold of his nose with your left hand and take a long thin probe.
And insert it in the middle of the nasal passage itself, while holding his nose straight because the vein does not appear to touch there; rather blood comes out from its side. And your hand should be steady with the probe a little bit and send what you need from the blood then bind him for a night because it dries quickly.
And as for the buboes, their benefit is in draining them for suffocation of the breath and beginning of scabies and black diseases which appear on the surface of the skin such as black boils, smallpox, bad ulcers and eczema. And how to do it: he binds a cord under them around his neck and the craftsman stands on the head of the patient.
The drunkard sits on a chair, then the sweat is raised to the length, widely and slightly, then the controlled bleeding or as much as you see of its need is scraped from it. Then this is done with the other sweat, and then the bandage is applied.
The two sows are tightened strongly, but not too tightly so that the drunkard does not suffocate. They are left overnight and the wound heals. And the sows of the armpits are useful. The sweat from them is applied after the sweating of the qifal, for it benefits from ulcers in the mouth and foul breath and poor wounds and the splits of the lips and bad wounds.
The one that is in the nose and around it. And how to sweat them, you make the drunkard sit in front of you and tie his neck with a turban, then turn his lips and look at the two sows which you see one on the right side of the lip and the second on the left side of her, and distinguish from them by their blackness.
And also around them are two black sows, you cut them into broken pieces. If it is difficult for you and you do not know what they are, go to the largest and most apparent of them, and do the same with the two sows in the lower lip.

And how to sweat them, you make the drunkard sit in front of you with his face to the sun, lift his tongue and look under it on one side a sow and on the other side a sow, their color being towards blackness.

And as for the three sows that are sweated in the armpit, they are the ones who were usually accustomed to being sweated among people generally. Their sweating is done on both sides.
Either insert a wide almond or olive twig into the joint, or cut with a thin iron lancet and it is this shape [and here is its likeness].
It is broad as you see, suitable for opening the prominent, swollen, apparent and visible veins that contain thick, dark blood. This is the likeness of the olive twig.
And this lancet is narrower and thinner, suitable for letting out the thin, clear veins that contain fine, yellowish blood. This is the likeness of the iron lancet.
This iron lancet is suitable for cutting, and there are kinds of broad and thin types according to the size and narrowness of the veins, and it can be used as a substitute for others, and among craftsmen it is well-known.
And as for the basilic vein, which is one of these three veins, the benefit of its severance lies in that it draws blood from the swellings that occur under the throat and neck, after the chest and belly. It is necessary for the one letting it out to warn him and be aware of it; because under it there is an artery, therefore if he erred and increased with the insertions of the lancet, it will cut this artery.
If blood flows out, therefore it is not suitable that its severance be with a lancet of insertion, but rather its severance be by cutting with the iron lancet. And if the basilic vein does not appear clearly, then beware and correct to another one or ask for some of its branch.
Or let out in its place the arm vein, for it is clear and cut it with the iron lancet as we said. And if you wanted to sever it by itself, then before taking hold of the arm, locate the spot until you identify the pulse location, then mark on it with ink.
Then tie the arm and cut the vein a gaping incision with a lancet as we have said, and try that the blow fall far away from the place of the artery. If you see when you tighten the bandage an inflation in the place which you were taught by the ink then that inflation is the swelling of the artery so avoid it.
If you see the blood at the puncture flow as urine flows and was thin red then know that it is from the arterial blood, so then hasten and place your finger on it for a long time then take your finger away, if the bleeding stops then stop it because it often stops, tie the arm.
Leave it, and warn the surgeon from neglecting it, let it be on his neck, nor move it for days until it heals. If the bleeding does not stop and overwhelms you and did not prepare a remedy in your presence then cut the artery if it appears to you, because its edges will contract and the bleeding will stop.
Or take a piece of bark, dry it and cut it in half, take one half and tie it firmly with the bandage and the pincers to another day, if the bleeding stops otherwise treat it as mentioned earlier by placing the hemostatic plugs for the bleeding, and cutting its blood is not difficult most of the time.
If the wound is small and the bandage can hold in the arm then inform him, but as for the deep vein which has a great benefit that it prevents drawing blood from the top of the head to the lower body to the place where it is composed of a branch of the saphenous and a branch of the femoral as we have said.
It is necessary for the surgeon to be on the neck of the patient, because under it is a nerve, if he increases the stitches with the lancet and hit the nerve an itching will occur in it which makes vision difficult and maybe did not heal at all, and this nerve often appears by feeling.
If the vein is thin and fine, so that it cannot be seen, you should make an incision in it with a lancet, and take great care not to touch the tendon. If the vein lies between two tendons, you should make an incision along its length. As for the frontal vein, the benefit of lancing it is that it draws blood from the head.
It is beneficial for the ailments of the eyes, and it should be lanced especially with a lancet made from olive wood or broad rhinoceros horn, because all veins are safe beneath it, as there is no artery nor nerve underneath.
Unless you must avoid with the lancet only the head of the muscle, and seek out the soft place; it does not harm him if he is not hit by the first blow, that he be lanced again until he bleeds.
But that swelling does him no harm. As for the manner of lancing, its complications and what should be done to repair it, first you must know that lancing is either used for the preservation of health only.
Or it is used for diseases, and if the lancing is used for the preservation of health and its continuation and to avoid the occurrence of diseases then it should be in one of the three veins in the armpit, namely the frontal vein, the saphenous vein or the basilic vein; and the lancing should be done at the beginning of spring if there are indications of congestion.
And the lancing should be done on Sunday or Tuesday after three hours in the morning. As for the lancing which is used for diseases, it does not have a limited time, but rather when necessity and need call for it, night or day.
And at any time, and every hour, and it is not to be lanced that the boys until they are fourteen years old, nor the elderly who have passed sixty years, if anyone resolves to lance for whatever reason.
The vessel should be cleaned before aspiration with a quill to remove any great clots, lest they draw towards the main organs when the vessel is aspirated. It should not be aspirated from one who is expected to swoon, or drunk, until he recovers.
Aspiration should also be avoided after the flow of menstruation and vomiting, regurgitation and excessive coitus, exertion and exercise, wakefulness and fasting and all physical or spiritual things that weaken the body. Then look at thinning the humors before that if the blood is thick.
With food, drink and medicines if possible. Then he enters the bath unless prevented by something or agrees to some exercise so that the blood is loosened and makes aspiration in the morning as we said and strives that his chest be free from all bad spiritual afflictions on that day.
The things that have bothered him, such as worry, anger and fear, and all physical afflictions like excessive fatigue and provocation and coitus and the like. And he prepares his surroundings with things which people have used habitually in using perfumes, aromatics, entertainment and the like, each person according to what is possible for him.
Then the aspirator sits on a pillow that is higher than the one on which the patient sits. Then he extends his arm and the aspirator grips it with his hand two or three times, then tightens the band with the cord and twists it two or three times, while the tension should be moderate.
Because the tension, if not moderate, is either excessive in the tightness preventing the flow of blood or excessive in relaxation also preventing the flow of blood. Then after the tightness he should rub the patient's hands with both his hands together tightly.
Until the veins swell and become palpable to the touch. Then he should wipe the aspirator's finger with old oil, especially, then put his index finger of the left hand on the same vein under the place where he wants to make aspiration a little so that the vein does not retreat from the blow. Because among the veins you may find one that clings to a needle during venesection, and there are some filled with pus. If you place the lancet on it, it will sink beneath the lancet and obstruct the vein; if it is opened, then the opening will be narrow.
Therefore, the lancet should be applied carefully and with caution in all these matters; then the lancet should be lowered. If the vein opens on the first attempt, it is well. Otherwise, the operation should be repeated once more beneath or slightly above that place by the rule of the screw, if the area does not become inflamed.
If the area becomes inflamed or boils, it should be left for a day or two and the bandage should not be tightened; it is possible that the abscess will have come forth and the patient will not need to go to the bath. The lancet may then be applied again if desired; if the needle puncture was small and the flow of blood was thin.
And you fear that the amount of blood that you want may not come out. So, place the lancet in the same hole and push it straight on; widen the opening a little bit and do this by the rule before the area becomes inflamed; because many people become inflamed when the small opening is made.
And if you see it to be inflamed, do not repeat it at all; because it will not benefit you in any way. So, put some liniment of olive oil on it; because it makes the blood flow easily and it is better for this place than olive oil itself or other ointments.
So, use liniment of olive oil in all your venesections for the veins when it is difficult to get the blood flowing. The compound of Farrooq and Shakhzinaya may also be used if one of them is placed on the place; because the blood will become liquid and flow easily if it was thick.
And if there is a large swelling in the place of the venesection, which often happens, especially to those who have not been cut except that one time or when the opening of the vein was small. So, proceed and put on the swelling a sponge soaked in warm water and salt; tighten it for an hour and it will dissolve.
You must do this after the blood has come out of the incision itself in its entirety or from another incision, if there remains something of blackness or greenish color in the place for a few days it will not harm. If you wish take some dissolved alum and vinegar over it.
Or something of fennel juice and the like. Often a swelling and inflammation occurs when you drain pus, so put your hand on it, if you feel it softening under your touch then that is bad inflammation, beware of putting anything from what we have mentioned over it.
Perhaps it has bled from an artery but you have clamped it with what is in it so that the place congealed, then treated him with other treatments until he recovers. You must take out of every person's blood according to his strength and as much as appears to be the dominant color of the blood.
If the blood is black let it come out until it becomes red, and similarly if you see it thick send him away until it thins, and similarly if it is warm send him away until it cools down. It is necessary for one who is full and strong and needs to remove blood all at once.
That the incision of the incisor be widened and the lancet be wide, and for one who is weak do the opposite of that, it is necessary to take him out in droplets and the hole be narrow. And what is best used in draining the incision is to be curved and rounded and slit-like, not a piercing.
And this type of incision is safe from bleeding and cutting a nerve, and it is better and safer than the incision by length and width. And for one who is accustomed to it when making an incision, it is necessary that you feed him something of soaked bread in rose water or syrup before making the incision.
If it is curved and you have let the blood out in three or four times, and if he has a bad temperament, then let him take before the incision something of soaked bread in wine or in honey mixed with rose water or in sweet rosewater.
If the abscess occurs in a place from which a large amount of blood has come out, it should be washed with water and the infusion of rosewater and be cleansed with rose oil. Its chest should then be sprinkled therewith. The rest of what we have mentioned about the separation should be used in its treatment under the chapter of abscess.
That which is due to purging, as for him who wanted to relieve his arm and let out its blood again, it should be done little by little according to the strength in consecutive days.
As for him who wanted to relieve his arm and let out its blood again, if his body was strong it should be done on the 7th or 8th hour from his first purging, as for him who wants to attract the blood from his body towards the side it has inclined to, he should do this in the second or third day.
As for him in whose body there is a lot of blood that has fermented, been inflamed and caused fever, it should be let out all at once, the quantity being great and the opening widened until the abscess is exposed after it having been made to meet all the conditions for purging. And to put your hand on his pulse while the blood is flowing, so that death does not occur in place of the abscess; for that often occurs if the purger has been ignorant and negligence has occurred. And it should not be done again if you wanted to relieve the arm and let out its blood a second time.
And if the mouth of the vein has become closed and the exit of the blood is obstructed, you should press on it with force or twist with strength; for that will make it very easy. Rather, leave it until it becomes purged again, or else draw out what has coagulated in the mouth of the vein with a lancet.
Or carry something of salt that has dissolved in water on it, or a piece of Faruq or Shakhzinai medicine and press on him with a gentle pressure until the blood comes out. If the vein has become inflamed then leave it alone.
Do not touch it until the swelling settles. If necessity compels letting out blood again, then either separate it above that location or separate it in the other arm or in another vein.
