And as for cutting the vein of the arm, it is cut in place of the eyelid and jugular if they are not found or are hidden because it is compounded from them. And how its incision is made: that he put his hand into warm water until the thread reddens and the vein appears distinctly then draw up over it with a band slightly tight in the middle.
Then cut the vein with a slight curvature, neither too wide nor too narrow; and let the incision be wide so that your cutting there is over the wrist a little. If it becomes difficult for the blood to come out, then return the hand to the basin with warm water and let the blood run in the water.
Until your need is met; and if you are in the days of summer, perhaps you will not have to return the hand into warm water again, and it is more that makes the blood run in the warm water in winter time. And cutting this vein is safer than all veins. Because there is no pulsating vein nor nerve under it. And as for cutting the saphenous vein from the right hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the liver. And how its incision is made: that you draw up the wrist with a band or your hand after putting it into warm water until the vein swells.
Because there is no pulsating vein nor nerve under it. And as for cutting the saphenous vein from the right hand, it is beneficial for ailments of the liver. And how its incision is made: that you draw up the wrist with a band or your hand after putting it into warm water until the vein swells. And it becomes distinct to the touch very much, then cut it with a slight curvature and if you severed it completely it will not harm him anything. Be cautious that you do not prolong your hand with the probe because under it is a nerve of the fingers and the place is exposed from flesh, then return the hand to the warm water.
And leave it to run the blood into it; because if you did not put it in warm water, the blood would freeze in the mouth of the vein and refuse to flow. And if you have extracted from it as much as your need requires, then place on the vein oil and salt so that it does not clot quickly.
And do likewise with every small branch. And as for its benefit, remove it from the left hand; it is beneficial for diseases of the liver. Likewise do you remove it, just like you did in the second (case), and as for removing the saphenous vein, its benefit is for diseases in the lower body such as ailments of the uterus.
And for amenorrhea and diseases of the kidneys, chronic sores on the thighs and legs and similar diseases. And how to remove it: that his leg be put in hot water and he carry on it a sponge until the veins appear, then tie over the joint with ligature, and the vein should be in place above the ankle visible towards the toe.
And it branches out into the face of the leg many branches, so remove it in the widest branch from it or at the ankle when it is together, for that is better and safer, and if you remove it in the face of the leg, the nerves which are under it will be preserved.
And make your incision crooked as if you want to amputate, and the forceps will be bent, and if it is difficult for the blood to come out, have him put his leg in hot water and leave the blood to run out until it is empty, because if the veins are cut with the incision at first time.
Then have him remove the vein upwards a little, because the place is safe and there will be no disaster if you preserved it from the nerve as we said, and likewise do with the saphenous of the other leg, whether it is the same or not. And as for the femoral vein, leave it in its place as we said at the groin on the inner side.
And the benefit of its removal for lumbar pain if it is that because of hot blood. And how to remove it: that you put in a hot water bath and hurry and tie his leg from below the hip up to above the ankle with four fingers with a thin long bandage, because it will not appear except like this.
If it appears, expose it on any case and keep it intact. Either straighten it, which is better, or cut it off with a cut or make an incision in it; for its place is sound. And in most people, it is very slight; if you do not find it.
If it did not appear at all, then expose some of its fibers; it is the one that appears on the back of the foot towards the heel and toes, and preserve them from the nerves and send out as much blood as you want.
Then dissolve the string, put a thread on the place of the incision and tighten the place; it will quickly recover.
Chapter 69.
In venesection and how to use it.
The venesection instruments can be made of horns, wood, copper or glass, and the venesection itself can be done in two ways: one with a condition, i.e. drawing blood, and the other without a condition.
And this venesection without a condition is of two kinds.
And the phlebotomy instruments of the axilla which are two plates of the neck from both sides, and the phlebotomy instrument of the jaw which is below the lower jaw from the mouth, and the phlebotomy instruments of the shoulders, and the phlebotomy instruments of the thighs, and the phlebotomy instruments of the calf bones.
Venesection is done by drawing blood from the tortuous veins spread throughout the flesh, and because of that the power of letting blood should not be wasted. And it is not suitable to use venesection with fire or without fire in any disease caused by a fullness, until the body has completely evacuated.
If necessity demands venesection either due to illness or habit, we have used it at the beginning of a month and at its end and in the middle and in any time that has passed.
For some people, if there is an abundance of blood in them to the point where it becomes necessary to extract it through venesection, they will find a heaviness and headache in their head. And among them are those who will find fullness and redness on their face and head and neck. And among them are those who will find hiccups in their chest and groin, and dimness and cloudiness in their eyes. And among them is one who will feel pain in the place of his incision.
And among them are those whose laughter is abundant. And among them are those who will find the taste of blood in their mouth, and their tongue will be heavy with it and it will become swollen. And among them are those whose sleep is excessive. And among them are those who see in their sleep blood, redness, kills, wounds, and things like that. Whenever we saw anything like that.
And especially if it is in the middle of the month, we have been ordered to perform venesection when this happens after the day has passed two or three hours. And as for the benefit of venesection with a needle, it benefits from heaviness in the head and water rising up to the eyes.
But it should be done after the body has been thoroughly purged. This venesection may be an alternative to letting out pus from a pustule. One should beware of using it on someone whose blood is cold or who has an inflamed condition, for it will cause him great harm.
Therefore it should not be used on the elderly and those whose heads are affected by cold diseases, and anyone who is addicted to it and it has caused him forgetfulness.
Therefore the phlebotomist should be ordered to lower his hand with the lancet a little down below, in fear that forgetfulness may occur. And as for venesection of the knee, it is an alternative to letting out pus from the eye and pustules on the eyelids.
Therefore, it is beneficial for a shortness of breath and tightness in the chest, congestion of the respiratory apparatus and coughing up phlegm, and fullness; one should raise a section from the knee slightly because if it were brought down below, it would produce weakness in the heart and stomach.
As for the venesection of the inguinal regions, it is beneficial for headaches and inflammation of the face, fever and constriction and pain in the roots of the teeth; it takes the place of bloodletting from the basilic vein. It should be ordered that the phlebotomist does not penetrate deeply with his hand lest he cut a vessel and cause bleeding.
As for venesection under the navel, it is beneficial for ailments in the mouth and decay of the gums and similar diseases that occur in the mouth; it takes the place of bloodletting from the jugular vein. As for venesection of the shoulders, it is beneficial from palpitations which arise from fullness and heat.
As for venesection of the flanks, it is beneficial from what benefits bloodletting from the three basilic veins because it attracts blood from all those superficial veins in the flesh and attracts those superficial veins from other thicker ones until it reaches the three thick veins.
It should be ordered that the phlebotomist does not penetrate deeply in the case because the place is spongy from flesh and under it are nerves and vessels. As for the single cupping glass which is applied on the varicose veins, it is beneficial from hemorrhoids and sores of the lower parts.
It should be ordered that the phlebotomist makes the cupping glass large and from copper because the place needs a strong heat and perhaps the glass of glass has broken and made a deep incision. As for the leg cuppings, they decrease fullness with great necessity.
Because it draws blood from the entire body and is beneficial for chronic pain in the kidneys, womb, and bladder, and brings on menstruation and is beneficial for pimples and boils. And it serves the purpose of cupping the sapernae veins and the podalic vessels except that it exhausts the body greatly and produces a state of lethargy in most people. The benefit of siphoning the podalic vessels is close to the benefit of bloodletting from the legs.
The method of placing the siphon is that the cup or vacuum should be placed first empty and with a moderate suction, nor shall you prolong the placing of the siphon but place it quickly and remove it quickly so that the body parts may eagerly accept it in a level manner. Then repeat this and follow it until you see the place has reddened and opened up.
And the redness of the blood appeared, then you must inspect and continue with a gentle suction little by little, then look at the condition of the body parts. And whoever among people has slender flesh and porous pores should be inspected only once otherwise the place may become ulcerated.
And the cupper is ordered to expand and deepen it a little and adjust the suction gently and with a soft movement. If there is in the blood a thickness, then he should be cupped twice. In the first instance, let him open up a path for the thin blood and its moisture, and in the second instance, let him investigate the extraction of the thick blood.
If the blood is very turbid, he should repeat the cupping three times to achieve the goal. And in general, if we want to extract a small amount of blood, one cupping is enough for us. If we desire to extract a lot of blood, we shall cup more than once. And if we predict that the blood is thick.
Then one should cup with a deep and moderate suction, up to the depth of the skin only. As for what is used as an ointment when placing the siphons, water, and what the patient should beware of. But whoever has thick, hard, and coarse skin with narrow pores.
The surfaces of the cauterizing irons should be smeared with ointments that are dissolved and mixed well, but if it is during summer, like anointing with camphor or borage or sweet orange or soaproot.
And if it is in the winter, like anointing with pansy or sops or verbena or lily and the like. And if the cauterizing iron is thick and cold, then let the ointment be of balsam or camphor or benzoin or spikenard and the like.
If the patient is someone whose flesh bleeds profusely, then one should refrain from applying ointment. And those are to be washed with rose water or cold water or fox grape water or soaproot water or radish water and the like. As for him whose blood is very wet.
Then he washes his cauterizing irons with vinegar or water of the mulberry and sumac and the like. And if his bleeding is thick, then he washes them with old wine or mercurial water or spikenard decoction or verbena and the like. It should be warned that bloodletting in a bathhouse and after bathing.
But it should be used after coming out of the bathhouse by an hour or two, and no one should sleep after bloodletting. What should be prepared for the patient before and after bloodletting.
He should first look at him, if the patient is bilious and has a tendency to feverishness and inflammation, then he should take cooling substances like pomegranate and henna with vinegar, rhubarb, beef or goat flesh that are dry-cooked, and dates and the like.
And if he is of a cold temperament, then he should drink honey water and date wine or dissolved spikenard and take moderate sweet wine that is in between the old and the new. And one should order little food and make his diet dry-cooked dishes, locusts and small birds, pale-cooked chicken, and to drink more beverage than food on the day of bloodletting and phlebotomy.
And it is fitting that some people be given a mixture of Farroq, musk or shiltha at times before venesection and bloodletting, or afterwards in order to strengthen the main organs and purify the blood. And it is not suitable for those who are already bleeding.
And as for the phlebotomy which is done without condition, it is the one that is placed on the liver and spleen and breasts and on the abdomen and back and place of the kidney and the hip because these organs cannot bear a condition on them. And it is intended by this to draw blood from organ to organ.
As for placing the catheter on the breasts in case of mastitis or we use it to eliminate a foul odor from an organ which has become putrid, just as placing the catheter on the abdomen and back, it cleanses the organ, warms it up and dispels the pain so that the bad smell may be eliminated.
And it is placed on the kidneys if a stone or gravel appears in them, by its means it may be drawn out and the stone or gravel may come out of its place. And likewise it does this when placed on the liver and spleen if a foul odor appears in them. And these catheters may also be used empty with only vinegar.
And it may be used with fire and it may also be used filled with warm water in cases of constriction, namely to fill the catheter so that it is large with hot water alone or with water that has been boiled with some herbs suitable for that.
Then it is placed filled on the place and held firmly in that position, left as such and repeated as needed. And this is the form of the catheter used with fire:.
Its mouth will be the size of two open fingers as we have depicted, and its depth is half a finger in width. And on one side of it, about half of that length, there is a small hole the size of which can accommodate a needle, made from thick-walled Chinese copper or yellow copper.
A flat, smooth cloth is placed on the part so as not to cause it any harm. There is a copper or iron wire in the middle of it where the candle is lit. This pad can be made large or small according to the nature of the disease.
This pad is often used; the embers of children and the young are not like those of men and women. As for how to put this pad on fire on the member, it is that you take a thread lit with fire from thick cotton or a small candle from flax and place it on the middle of the rod which is in the middle of the pad.
The fire rises upwards towards the bottom of the pad so that it does not burn the body of the cauterizer. Then it is placed on the member and the finger is put over the hole we mentioned, until we hold the pad without needing to remove the finger.
And the steam came out through that hole and the pad dissolved in place. Then the wick is lit again on the shape and it is returned if it was needed, but the pad which is used for the disease of drowning by water does not have a rod inserted nor a hole.
And it is used only by filling with water and placing on the member alone. And this is its shape:.
And this pad, whenever it was large so as to hold a lot of water, was better.
The Seventh and Ninetieth Chapter.
For the treatment of epilepsy.
The cauterizer is used most of the time in members which cannot be put caustic on, either because of their smallness like the mouth and lips, etc. or because the member is separated from the flesh, such as the finger and nose, etc.
And as for the manner of its use, you take from the epilepsy which is in the pure and clear sweet water free from impurities then leave it overnight until tomorrow in the same sweet water until it becomes dry and nothing remains inside. Then the body is first purged by venesection or bloodletting, then the affected member is cleaned.
Until it turns red, then a poultice is placed on it. If it becomes filled and the scab falls off, and if possible, the area is smeared with resin; otherwise, the area is washed with vinegar, then with much water, rubbed and pressed. If bleeding continues after the scab has fallen and this happens to be an oozing discharge, a linen cloth is dipped in cold water.
And it is placed on top until the discharge stops. If much blood comes, powder of zaja or alum or something like it from the aloe is sprinkled on it until the bleeding stops; or half crushed powdered galbanum is put on the area.
And it is left until the galbanum adheres to the area. If the bleeding stops, if there is a need to reapply the scab, do not reattach that same scab if possible; if the scab refuses to adhere, the area is washed with warm blood.
Or a needle is inserted into the area until something comes out of the blood, then it is left. If it feels any more blood, it adheres to the place; if you want it to fall off, sprinkle some vinegar or salt or ashes on it; then it falls off.
The Third Chapter.
In fractures.
This chapter is also very urgent and necessary for the practice of medicine, which is treating fractures and dislocations that occur in bones.
Know, my sons, that this chapter will be claimed by the ignorant among physicians and common people, as well as those who have never reviewed an ancient book about it nor read a single word from it.
And due to this reason, in our country, this art from the sciences has been non-existent and I have never found a single person who practiced it at all. And I have benefited from it as much as I have benefited from the lengthy reading of the first books, and I have been diligent in understanding them until I extracted the science of this subject from them. Then I followed practice and experience throughout my life.
And I have included everything that pertains to my knowledge in this chapter. And my practice has followed it after I have brought it closer to you and simplified it, extracted from the complexities of length, shortened it to utmost shortness and clarified it in utmost clarity. And I have represented for you many images of the tools that are used in it.
For it is an increase of clarity, as I have done in the preceding chapters, and there is no strength but by Allah, the Most High, the Great.
The First Chapter.
In it are brief summaries and generalities regarding the breaking of bones, which should be presented first. And before we start mentioning each severed limb one by one in this chapter, it is necessary to mention here a summary or a number of sections. You will first be compelled to understand it and grasp its reality, you yourselves and anyone else eager to learn this noble craft.
I say that whenever one of a break, dislocation, fracture or fall occurs, it is necessary to hasten first to remedy it or alleviate it or all of them altogether. If an obstacle like weakness of strength prevents that, or the thing that happened was something of that sort - a young child or an elderly person in great haste - or if the time was extremely hot or extremely cold indeed.
Then his food is restricted to cold beans and the flesh of birds and fish, and he is prevented from drinking and eating heavy meats and overindulging in food, and every nourishment that fills the veins with blood, until if the swelling subsides and does not expect bleeding into the place.
Then the healer should return to his first treatment, which he was accustomed to do. If the fractured bone is given in a broth, then it is necessary for the healer to feed himself with foods that are nourishing, thick and pure, such as barley porridge, rice, heads of grains, vegetables and cow dung. And eggs, fresh fish and thick drinks, and similar things. By this treatment the union of the fracture will be quicker and more complete. Know that fractured bones, if they are in strong young men or elderly people, it is not possible for them to become connected and united again as they were originally.
The joints of their bones and its softness, so it can become united and connected again as the bones in very young children. But nature grows on the broken bone from all sides something similar to glue, thick, sticking and binding them together until they are firmly connected to each other.
And binds some of it together until it comes to be in a state of great strength and firmness, as it was originally, so that nothing can hinder its functions.
And for this reason it is necessary to make the patient's food nourishing, thick and gluey as we said. Know that the types of fractures differ according to the difference in the organs because a fracture of the tibia bone is different from a fracture of the skull bone and a fracture of the chest bone is different from a fracture of the spine.
And also all the other organs are different from each other, and we will come to mention every type of fracture in its own chapter, detailed and separated from others. And a fracture of bone can differ itself as well.
Because it may be a break that has splintered without causing any fragments, and it may be a break along the length of the bone with fragments and protruding or unprotruding pieces. And the fracture can be accompanied by an injury and incision in the skin, and it can be a slight shock to the bone.
For every type of fracture, there is a particular trick in reduction as will be mentioned in its place. And what characterizes the breaking of bone are its deformity and twisting and sensitivity to touch, and grating sound when you rub it with your hand.
But when there is no apparent deformity, grating sound or sensitivity to touch in the place of fracture, and when you press the bone with a little trouble or discomfort, it is not a fracture but a bruise, slight break or slight dislocation. Do not move it at all by stretching or rubbing.
But instead, take some of the medicines that will be mentioned afterwards to suit the place and then bind it with a gentle grip. Know that if a bone breaks into two pieces without any fragments remaining, then each piece has separated from its companion.
You should begin to set it straight and make it smooth from the beginning before a hot swelling occurs. If a hot swelling does occur, leave it for some days until the swelling subsides, then shape it in any way you can with ease and trickery.
Know that setting and smoothing it is easier than the bone with fragments. Then tie as will be mentioned afterwards. And if the bone has fragments, you must stretch the broken limb from both sides with your hands or legs.
Either with your hand if the limb is small, or with two straps, or with a strap and your hand. Let the limb be placed on its natural position on a flat place until the separated parts of the broken bone are stretched out. Then remove those protrusions in their places by any means you can with trickery and ease.
Make every effort that your action does not cause pain or discomfort to the patient, and make sure to press one of the bones firmly back onto its companion in the best possible manner. At that time, touch them and feel with your hand, if you see something contrary, correct it and shape it as much as your ability allows.
Be cautious of the severe compression and powerful squeezing, as many ignorant people do, and often change the temperature in the part as I have observed them doing repeatedly.
Then, apply the part in a state of calmness and relaxation after its adjustment and precision, and warn the physician to move it during his patient's wakefulness, sleep, changes, movement, urination, and all other movements that he is capable of.
That he may, when feeling pain or discomfort in his previously set position of the part, move it to another position where he does not feel pain with it, and examine this other position so that it is level and straight.
So that the part may not be crooked if it becomes numb, and as for how to tightly bind a broken limb, it is what I have described to you. Know that fractured parts vary in size, whether large or small, and their shape; those which are smaller such as the arm, finger, hand and similar ones.
So the bandages of the fractured parts should be soft and pliable, but those which are larger such as the thigh, back and chest should have tight and firm bandages because a wide binding is necessary for large limbs and pulls them tightly from all sides with equal tension, without any defect or unevenness.
Then you start after finishing the adjustment to apply the plaster suitable for it. You start with a gentle pressure on the place of the fracture, then you begin to roll the bandage onto the same place of the fracture itself three or four turns as the part deserves, and bind your hand slightly with the bandage, then take it to the upper side of the place of the fracture.
With less tension than what you had for the fractured place, then it stretches away from the fracture a little and relaxes the tension a little until it takes something good from the correct place, then take another roll and wrap it also on the fractured place.
Rolls of linen or cloth to wrap around it, then take the rolled linen and go down to the lower part of the fracture. Let your action in tightening and softening the rolled linen be as mentioned for the upper one, then put between the rolls of the same kind of material, whether it is linden or rag, what will match with the deformity of the fracture.
If there is a deformity, do not put anything in it; otherwise, wrap another bandage on top of it, then arrange over these tight rolls when you are sure that there is no inflation or hot swelling within the member.
If there is an inflation or a hot swelling, take on it what will calm down that swelling and go away with the inflammation, and leave it for some days; then, tighten over it these rolls, let them be from half of split bamboo, cut and well-prepared wisely, or be from wooden splints.
The ones that are made from cedar or palm tree sheets or khilanj or kalak and similar things that were prepared from those, and let each roll be of the same size and this shape itself.
Unless the roll that is placed on the fracture itself be thicker and a little wider than the other rolls, and as for the length of the roll it should be made according to what suits the broken member whether big or small. Shape of the roll:.
Then tie on the rolls with another bandage according to your first tightening by itself, then tie from above with tight threads according to what we mentioned about the tightening and that it be more tightened on the place of the fracture. And as far as you are away from the fracture, the tightening should be less.
And it should be that the threads are of medium thickness and softness, and they should be from wet cotton thread, especially because the threads if they were thick like what you have seen that foolish people do by making their threads from unfolded cotton strips, which is a great error.
Because it leads the tightness outside the balance, and also the thin threads do not fit because you will not reach with the tightness what you want, nor should be between the stitches less than a finger.
If the patient is injured by the edges of the stitches after being tightened in the correct places, place a layer of soft linen or wool under them until he is no longer harmed by that. And if the break was with an injury and there has been a tear in the skin, we will come to mention it separately.
Know that not every broken member should be stitched with stitches from the first day, because if the member is large it is not right to put stitches on it until after five days or seven or more according to your safety from occurring hot inflammation.
And this is the type of compresses that the ancients used to bind with, which are placed on the break and fracture and swelling, a common general compress that binds the break and suits most members especially children and women because it does not weigh heavily or grow cold.
And it is that you take from the dust of the millstone, which is the bottom of fine flour that adheres to the edges of the millstones when the mill is moved, then you knead it as it is without grinding it with the whiteness of eggs, and make its dough neither thick nor thin, then use it.
Type of another compress that binds the break, and the amputation, and the swelling, which is taken from the ashes of a cow, and aladen, and aqua cake, and the resin, and the soap of each one ten dirhams, and myrrh of each one five dirhams, and twenty dirhams from the Armenian clay or the Roman. They are all pounded and mixed with water of aloe or the whiteness of eggs if the temperament of the patient is disturbed, then this compress is used because it has a good composition that binds broken bones quickly and suits most people because of its moderation.
Description of the last ointment which is also useful for fractures and contusions. Take from each of ten drachms of myrrh, marshmallow and white hyacinth ten drachms of rose water five drachms of Armenian clay twenty drachms; pound all together very finely, mix or knead with water or egg whites and use.
Description of an ointment for joints and movable bones which are displaced from their places, and it calms the pain that opposes them, and it is useful for dislocation of a bone and fracture. Take wool that has been cleaned, immerse it in vinegar and cooked oil and put it on the place; this ointment does not have the strength to set.
But it excels in calming the hot swelling and driving pains, especially. Description of another ointment which sets a broken bone. Take the skin of unripe figs and the leaves of wild hellebore, pound all together and plaster with them moistened. Description of another ointment which is brief and used when splinters enter fractures.
And you want to analyze the remaining swelling, take from each of the white henna and rose petals and the dried jasmine one part; pound all together and knead with paint if the member is not inflamed, but if it is inflamed then knead with water of celery or with water and use.
Description of another ointment stronger in analysis than the first one which is used when a hard swelling occurs at the time of splintering of the bone. Take from each of the white henna, walnut, opium poppy, jasmine, rose petals and pomegranate peel one part.
Pound all together and knead with vinegar or sweet water or with paint; all that according to the warmth of the member and the quietness of its state. And as for Galen, he did not mention in his book that it should be placed on the broken bone when setting it.
Unless the ointment made from fat and oil, used only, is applied. The description is that it should be of a medium thickness between hardness and softness. And Galen saw that it was placed on the broken limb when it was bound with things containing linings such as resin, myrrh, and saffron, like what we mentioned.
The description of a poultice that is useful for weakness and pain, taken from garlic, fenugreek, human hair that has been singed or bird down, camel's heel and salt in equal parts, crushed and sifted and mixed and applied with it. And the amount of bandage that should remain.
Then it is dissolved, and if there has not been in the place severe pain or cracking or movement of the broken bone from its place, then do not dissolve it for many days because an intense crackling or sharp pain or swelling may result.
So act quickly and dissolve it at the time, and take away the poultice. Then take a moist green cloth or a wet sea sponge and immerse it in warm water and wash the place with it until the crackling and pain settle down. Then leave the member to rest for an hour.
Then place on it the scented wool that has been soaked in vinegar and oil or rose ointment and bind it for a night until the hot swelling settles down, the inflammation goes away, and the pain is gone. Then return it to gentle stretching and mild poultice, without drawing it with your initial tightness.
And treat it gently until it heals. If you see that the swelling, redness, pain and inflammation and all the symptoms have gone completely and required the poultice and stretching, then return to doing as I did initially, whether it happens or not.
Do not dissolve it except after three or four or five or seven days, and it may be left for twenty days altogether, all of this as you see the condition of the member as we said until if the fracture necessitates and approaches the union of flesh on it.
When the break becomes swollen more than your first tightness, increase also the thickening of the patient's food as has been previously mentioned. If you see that the place of the fracture is dry and loose more than it should be, know that the food is prevented from reaching it.
Wash it with warm water at every time it heals, let this be in every three days and slightly ease the tightness, for by doing so nourishment is supplied to the member and it quickly heals. As for what the ignorant craftsmen make from resetting the broken member once more.
If it doesn't heal first as it should and heals with a twist, then that is an error on their part and a great fault. And if it was correct, they would have mentioned it in their books and implemented it, and I haven't seen any of them in this matter to be noticeable. The truth is that it should not be done at all.
The Second Chapter.
In the fractured bone of the head.
The types of fractured bones in the head are numerous and their forms varied, and their causes diverse. Some fractures occur from a blow with a sword, and it can be that the bone is cracked all the way to the serous membrane which lies beneath the bone. As does a wedge in wood.
And therefore this type of fracture is called a wedge, and it can also be that the sword cut some of the bone and cracked its surface only without penetrating to its end, and this type of fracture is called a clean break. And the wound of these two fractures can be either large or small.
And there is a type of fracture that is cracked or split, and its cause is a blow with a stone or falling on a stone or the like. This type of fracture can be either deep so it has approached the membrane under the bone, or it can be on the surface of the bone.
A wound of these two fractures is either large or small, and some fractures are hidden in the bone of the head of hair and it is a slight crack.
So this type of fracture is called hair-like, and from it a fracture occurs over a pebble or stone that falls into the bone page inside and becomes inflamed like lead if it is struck so that part of it enters inside.
And it happens more often in the moist bone of the head like the skulls of young boys, and for all these types of fractures there are exposed and unexposed fragments, and we will come to mention the treatment of this all in its place, and we identify all these types of fractures by examining them.
And inspection by probes and extracting the rotten flesh from them, and as for the hair-like type it is recognized that by exposing on the bone and wiping and smearing on it with ink then the fracture appears black, and as for the treatment of the fracture one looks first to the symptoms of the patient.
For if you see from his symptoms something that is clearly indicative of fear such as vomiting blood and its spread, the departure of the intellect, the cessation of speech, the coma, the sudden fever, the dullness of the eyes and their redness and similar symptoms, do not approach him or treat him because death is certain for him with these symptoms in most cases.
And if you see symptoms that do not frighten you and hope for his safety, then at that time take him in treatment, which is that if the wounded person comes to you first when he was injured and it was during the winter days.
You should strive to remove the bone before the fourteenth day in any case, and if it is during the summer days you should hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day before what is under the bone becomes rotten and those symptoms we described are exposed.
If the bone fracture has reached the membrane covering the brain and is withered and rotten, then it must be cut off the portion of the fractured bone that I have described to you, which is to shave the head of the wounded patient.
And you expose the bone in any way that is within your power and according to the shape of the wound, what may be hidden from the patient. If it is exposed bleeding or a hot swelling to you, then face this with what is appropriate.
And it is that you stuff the place with poultices soaked in rosewater, oil and saffron and close the wound until the swelling subsides and the bleeding ceases. Then take to covering the bone and removing it, which will be one of two actions.
But the single action is that you cut the bone with a fine sharp blade, and this is its picture:.
Then you use another cutting tool after this one, wider than it by a little bit, and this is its picture.
Then you also use another cutting tool, even wider than the second one, and it is necessary that you have a number of different tools, some wider than others, and some shorter than others, with extremely sharp edges, made from Indian steel or good iron.
And use a gentle instrument to strike the cutting tool so that you don't shake the head and harm it. If the bone is strong and hard, then it must be perforated around it before using the cutting tools with the non-sinking drills which they called non-sinking drills.
Because it doesn't exceed the limit of the skull's bone to what is behind it, so that the drill has a curved groove on what is below its sharp head, similar to a small ring or circle that prevents it from sinking and exceeding the thickness of the bone.
And it is necessary for you to take a number of these drills, each suitable for the thickness of that bone until they prepare you for every skull with its edge sharp in length and shortness equivalent to the thickness of that skull. These are images of three types. There are three types of wounds (muthaqib): large, medium and small.
As for making a hole around the fractured bone, it is that you place the awl on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that the bone has perforated, then move the awl to another place and make after each hole a space of the thickness of the probe or approximately.
Then you cut with the chisels what is between every two holes and do this to the extent that you can be gentle on it as we mentioned until the bone either comes out by hand or by something else from some of the tools which I have prepared for this.
Like the file and the smooth chisels, and you must be extremely careful that neither the awl nor the chisel touch the peritoneum. If you have loosened the bone and freed it from the peritoneum which was stuck to it and extracted it, then you should clean it.
And you smooth the roughness that remains in the bone with another tool that resembles the chisel, but it must be finer and softer than all the chisels. If any small bones or splinters remain, then take them gently with what you have of tools.
Then you treat the wound after that with lint and ointments which will be mentioned, and as for the other side of the work it is very easy and far from harm, about which Galen has spoken and praised greatly. His saying is: It is incumbent on you first to start by exposing a part of the bone from the place where it was broken more and more, until if you have exposed that part, you have shaped under it the end of this chisel-like awl and its shape is as follows:
The chisel-like awl part is smooth and does not cut anything, and the pointed part of it in its longitudinal sides as you see to be on the bone, then the chisel-like awl is struck from one side.
With a small mallet until it cuts all the bones gently as it turns, and you are safe from the membrane which will not happen to be a terrible accident. If the surgeon were one of the most ignorant and obstinate people, yes, even if he was sluggish.
So if something remains from the membrane on some places of the bone, scrape it away with the edge of the file itself and relieve him gently; then he will be relieved from it without harm or fear.
And as for the bone fracture that has not reached the membrane and a part of the bone has been cut off while there was roughness and small fragments left in it, you should strip away this roughness and extract these fragments with smooth files that have been made from them with different degrees so that you can use them in every place more appropriately and improve them as your work leads you.
And with the same fractured bone and its shape, you should first use in stripping the broadest of these files then afterwards the finer one, and you keep doing that on the bone until you reach using the finest and most delicate ones.
And as for the small hair-like fractures and slight breaks, you should treat each one separately according to what leads to its improvement. This is something that does not escape from anyone who has in this craft the slightest insight into the knowledge we have written and shown about the large breaks.
If the membrane remains exposed when you are removing the bone, it is necessary that you take a cotton rag on the size of the wound and soak it in rose oil and put it on the mouth of the wound then take another folded or trisected cotton rag. And soak it in sherbet and rose oil, and put it on the first cotton rag, and do that as lightly as you can so that you don't burden the peritoneum. Then use it from above a broad band and tighten it only to hold the wound.
Let him be treated with this remedy for a day or two, until the swelling is reduced and it heals. Then use the medications that are beneficial for dryness, such as roots of senna and delicate rose petals, and Daeq al-Kundur and Zarand and similar ones. And make powder from these medicines.
And sprinkle on the wound when dry, in general use a medicine that can dry and not fester. Exert your effort when treating an injury that it remains clean without fat or pus or allow blood to clot in it whatsoever.
For if the pus accumulates on the membrane of the brain, it corrupts and infects it, and a great damage occurs to the patient, and it can cause an abscess in the skull's vault when it is exposed and the bone becomes uncovered, especially if neglected in its treatment.
If the skin of his forehead is black, look at it; if he shows the symptoms we mentioned, know that he is doomed to die. And if the darkness was because a medicine was put on him and had the power to cause this darkness.
It is necessary to take a part from honey and three parts from rose oil, crush them well together, smear the mixture on a cloth, then place it on the peritoneum, then treat him with various treatments until he recovers.
The Third Chapter.
For treatment of a broken nose.
Know that the upper part of the nose does not break except both or one of them, for they are bony and the lower part is cartilaginous and it does not break but rather becomes inflamed, crooked and disfigured.
If one of its parts breaks, you should insert a small finger into the nostril and set that break from within with your thumb and index finger from outside until the nose returns to its natural shape; do this gently and make sure not to cause the patient any pain or distress.
If the fracture is at the top of the nose and a finger has not been applied to it, then it should be set with the edge of an awl that is slightly thicker. If the fracture is on both sides, do the same. Let him begin to bind it in the first day from the fracture.
If it is possible, otherwise after the seventh or tenth day at quiet of the swelling, then a thread from flaxen cloth is put into the hole of the nose. If the fracture is on one side, one thread is put in, and if the fracture is on both sides, two threads are put in.
Let the thread be thick to the extent that it fills the hole of the nose. Some of the first bandagers have mentioned that the thread should be smeared with fat and changed every day, but I do not see this, rather it is necessary to smear the thread with white eggs moistened with gum arabic.
Then the thread is left until the bone sets and the cartilage hardens. And a place where the thread was in the nose can be entered with quill reeds of the goose after they have been wrapped with linen cloth, so that its blockage is for the fracture of the nose more severe lest the nasal passage becomes obstructed to breathing.
But this is not necessary, if you want to do it and if you want to make threads. If there is swelling in the nose during your work and a warm tumor, then compress the nose with linen cloths or with soaked cottons in oil of roses or red or something from the ointment of diachylon.
If there is no swelling, then it should be compressed from outside with semolina powder and crushed gum arabic which has been mixed with white of eggs. Then a linen pad is placed on it without binding the nose to anything at all. If the bones of the nose have broken into small fragments.
Or it has been shredded, then sewn on and removed with the instrument that is suitable for it. Then the incision should be sutured and treated with what will heal and cover from appropriate ointments. If there was a wound inside the nose, it should be treated with threads and lead pipes are used. Until he recuperates.
Chapter Four.
In the treatment of lower lip fractures.
If the lower lip has been fractured without a wound, and it was only broken from outside and has not split in two and become loose inside then its recognition is easier.
If the fracture is in the right side, then you should put your left thumb into the patient's mouth and do likewise if the fracture is on the left side. Then you should lift up the fractured part from inside gently to outside.
And with the other hand from outside, control it and correct its alignment. If the jawbone has cracked in two, then a splint should be applied to both sides until its alignment is corrected. If it occurred in the teeth, they will re-emerge.
Or they will spread apart, so stitch what you find of them with gold or silver thread or catgut, then place a compress on the broken lip, and then place over it a large tight dressing or a piece of leather the length of the lip. Then tie it from above as you see fit to tie it and agree with his compression so that it does not become undone. And command the patient to rest and be calm, and make his food a liquid diet. If you suspected that something has changed in shape in one of its forms then intervene by treating it on the third day.
Then tie it from above as you see fit to tie it and agree with his compression so that it does not become undone. And command the patient to rest and be calm, and make his food a liquid diet. If you suspected that something has changed in shape in one of its forms then intervene by treating it on the third day. Then you will repair what is dislocated with the powder of the millstone mixed with egg white or fine flour after removing the compress from it, and place over the dressing a gentle liquid bandage. As long as that dressing sticks to him and has not changed for the bone's condition then leave it alone.
Do not treat it until he recuperates and the fracture gets stronger, for often this fracture strengthens in three weeks. If during that time an inflamed swelling appears then use what we mentioned repeatedly to soothe it until that swelling disappears.
As for the case where the fracture is accompanied by a wound, I have looked into it. If the bone fragment is small or there are several fragments, smooth out the removal of those fragments as agreed upon with the instrument's extraction. If the jaw's opening is narrow, widen it with a probe to the extent you need.
Then if those fragments have been extracted and there is nothing left of them, draw a line on the jaw's opening. If it is wide then there is no concern, but if not, place one of the dressings which are suitable for this over it. And bandage the wound until it heals.
The Fifth Chapter.
For treating a fractured humerus.
More often the humerus fractures in front of the shoulder and it breaks into one of three forms. Either it fractures and is crushed by two without causing fragments, which is easier to treat.
Or else fragments are caused in the break, which is more difficult to treat. Or it breaks with an open wound. In a case where there is no wound from the break, have two servants ready. One should secure the arm that follows the broken humerus and the other extend the neck towards the opposite side.
Then smooth out the break with your fingers until its shape is as it should be and there are no knots or lumps in it. If you need to extend further, place a ball of wool or cotton under the patient's armpit.
And its bone will be as you need it. Extend and raise the humerus and press the ball with your hand until the break is smoothed out as it should be. If you are unable to pull the end of the humerus towards the outside because it has sunk in, then have the patient lie on his chest.
And place a medium-sized sponge under his shoulder in the bone. Have the servant press down on his shoulder until the humerus that has sunk in rises up. Then correct the break and smooth it out with your fingers.
If you feel that a fragment of the ulna has been fractured and it is moving, you should make an incision on it and carefully remove that fracture. If the fracture was lodged in the bone then prepare one of the cutting instruments I have mentioned for its removal. After it is under the ulna, the instrument which holds the peritoneum, made of wood or iron.
It is similar to a spoon that has no flaw and its width will be according to what you need from the size of the bone, large or small. As for its length, it will also be according to what you can do with it. It should have two ends, one wider and the other narrower.
If the wound that you made when extracting the bone fragment is wide and you have secured the hot swelling, then join the edges of the wound with stitches. And if the wound is slight or you feared the hot swelling, then broaden the wound and its margins as much as you made the incision.
If the swelling is hot, then treat it with the incision after applying rose water and vinegar or wine to it. And if you want to set the bone without any wound or cut, then apply a plaster made from pumice powder mixed with egg white. You place a soft pad on it then put a ball under his armpit if needed, then take a long turban that is about an arm's length or around that much in width. Then apply a folded bandage over the pad and plaster, and then take a thin splint. It should be three fingers in width and also in length, then insert it into a cloth, then insert that cloth with the splint into the place on the turban where the fracture is located, then tie the turban around the fracture as you turn. And tie it around his healthy neck and his sick neck, wrap it around him several times on each side. As you see the tightness of the tie setting the fracture firmly in place, and this is not hidden from you.
And it is necessary that the splint not be removed from the broken bone. And for that, the attendant should check on him every day; whenever the bandage has relaxed and he sees that the splint has been removed, he should correct it and tighten the bandage. Then let the attendant sleep on his back with a small cushion under his armpit at night.
So let it be raised with his arm so that the fracture of the humerus is elevated by the height of the shoulder or let his arm be tied to his neck, and do not remove the bandage unless an incident occurs from a scratch or swelling in the place for twelve days.
Then renew the dressing if you see a face for that. It is reapplied with compression and left until it consolidates and the fracture of the humerus heals, and it becomes stronger and firmer in twenty-eight days. And there may be some people in less than that.
The Sixth Chapter.
Regarding the treatment of a fractured shoulder, it is rare for the shoulder itself to break in the wide part and that what breaks are its fragments. So if there is a fracture or it is broken in its middle and this can only be known by touch, then according to what shape the fracture is formed from.
You fix it and apply to its natural shape from all sides by whatever trick you can, then place on the place dust of the handstone with whitening of eggs and the smooth cloth, and put over a broad splint from a board.
Thin according to the size of the shoulder all or a little wider, if there is swelling under the splint in one of the places of the shoulder, fix that swelling with a smooth cloth until the splint descends evenly, then tighten from above with a long headband.
Tighten firmly and secure the splint so that it does not move from its place, and check the bandage every day; whenever the bandage has relaxed tighten it again and reapply the splint if it has moved away from its place. And let the attendant sleep on his correct side, and the shoulder will heal in twenty days.
On the 25th day, if this number is complete, then untie the ligature and you will be safe. For it is one of the bones that are not subject to fracture or dislocation, if a splinter protrudes from the bone and is hidden under the skin, cut over it and remove it. Make what I mentioned in the treatment of a fractured ulna for soothing the inflamed swelling, if something like that happens.
Chapter Seven.
For treating a broken chest.
The chest may break in the middle and rarely will this be exposed, but its edges are more likely to break and splinter. And a sign of it if it breaks in the middle is that it inclines downwards and exposes great pain and difficulty in breathing and coughing.
And perhaps blood will flow and it will be apparent that the bone is splintered and not felt, to treat it let the patient lie on his back and a cushion should be placed between his shoulders then compress his ribs with both hands from each side and soothe him as much as possible in straightening him out on all sides.
And agree him until his bone shape returns to what is proper, then place a poultice and a compress on him and put over it a splint of a wooden board from a palm tree or a walnut etc. in softness after it has been wrapped in a cloth. Then soothe it in binding it on the broken bone so that it does not come off, and pass the bandage over to the back many times and tighten it tightly then check the bandage at all times, and every time it relaxes tighten it.
And if necessity calls for its removal in the place of ulceration or pain or swelling then proceed to remove it and take off the poultice and treat what is exposed from that in a way of treating it then return the poultice when you see fit. Face it and bind him tightly until he is clean.
The Eighth Chapter.
In the correction of ribs, if they have been broken, know that the breaks are in the rough places which follow the back and their extremities from before, only exposing to them the wound because they are spongy.
And correct them by straightening the break with the fingers on a firm face until it becomes even in shape as it should, then apply a salve and bind the broken bone with a splint if necessary. If the rib fracture leans towards the inside, it is exposed to much pain and severe suffering.
And the pharynx like the pharynx which is exposed to someone who has a hawking, in order that the bone be exposed and also difficulty breathing and coughing, and spitting up a lot of blood, this is a difficult affliction to treat, and the ancients have found many ways to deal with it.
And among them who said that it is necessary to make the food of the patient something that will produce belching and gas, so that the belly swells and the break protrudes outward, but we dislike this in case it confirms the occurrence of the hot swelling if it has not occurred. If it has occurred then it increases and confirms it.
And some of them said that a compress should be placed on the place, then it is squeezed strongly and this is similar to probing except that he is afraid that the compress will draw in pus into the place because of its weakness. And some of them said that the place should be covered with wool that has been immersed in hot oil.
And a poultice is made between the ribs until it is filled, then the bandage will be even if turned to roundness. Then the patient is treated with the treatment of hawking from food and medicine, and if the patient becomes exhausted it is a severe matter that cannot be tolerated. The bone was corroding the membrane in a noxious manner and it caused us to be afraid of the patient. So, we should incise on the place and uncover the fractured limb then we shape under it the instrument which preserves the peritoneum which its description is mentioned above and we should remove the bone gently and take it out.
Then we should join the edges of the wound if it is large with suturing and treat it with ointments until it heals, if a hot swelling appears in the meantime then hasten before the inflammation by applying onion juice to smear on the place and treat the patient by what calms the swelling from inside as well.
And he should lie on the side that the sleep escapes him until it heals.
The Ninth Chapter.
For the cure of the boils of the back and neck.
As for the bones of the neck, if a fracture occurs to them and rarely is this exposed to them and more often are the abscesses exposed to it, as well as the vertebrae also; if that is exposed to one and you wanted to know whether it will heal or not. So look, if you saw that his hands were stiff and numb and dead and he was not able to move them or stretch them or flex them, and if you had pressed them or punctured them with a needle and he did not feel it and did not find pain in them then know that in most cases he will not heal, it is lost.
And if he was able to move them and felt the numbness and stiffness, then know that the marrow of the bone has escaped and the patient will heal with treatment; and if a boil on the back had occurred like that and you wanted to know whether it would also heal or not.
Look at his legs, if you saw that they were stiff and in them occurred what happened to the hands then; if he lay on his back the wind and stool came out without will, and if he lay on his stomach the urine came out without will. If he lay on his back and wanted to urinate, but could not do so, know that he is doomed. Do not bother treating him unless nothing of the sort happens to him. The treatment for what has happened in this case is to attempt to subside the hot abscess.
That you put on the inflamed lymph nodes rose oil alone or with fried egg yolks, place it three times a day until the hot abscess settles. Then take one of the moistened compresses and tie it up with a bandage, instructing him to be calm and still.
And he should not sleep except on the side where he does not feel pain. If a piece of bone or something was to break off at the place of the abscess, it is necessary that you cut through his skin and take out that bone. Then gather the edges of the wound, if it is large, with sewing. Then treat it with ointments until he gets better. If the last bone of the coccyx was to break, which is the sacrum, then it is necessary that you insert the thumb from the left hand into the pelvis.
And repair the broken bone with the other hand as you are able and finish the repair. Then put on the compress and gauze if necessary, then tie it up.
If you feel a piece of bone that has broken loose, cut into it and remove it. Then treat the wound with the treatment mentioned above until he gets better.
The Tenth Chapter.
For treating a fracture of the hip.
Say what breaks the bones of the hip, if it broke then it is only a crack that disperses at its ends and fractures in length and tilts towards the inside and exposes to cold and pain in the place and swelling and numbness in the leg which the break occurred in.
If you are going to operate on a bone, pass your hand over it until you can feel the break. If the break is only in its extremities, so fix the break as best you may, so that it resembles its natural form.
If the break is in its length or it has bent inward, place the sawyer on his belly until you can prepare to mend the break. If you have mended it, apply a compress and then put on him a splint from wood or skin and tighten it tightly.
Do not be afraid for him to have the break move or the splint to come loose, and fill in the gaps with what fills them until it becomes taut evenly. Order the sawyer to sleep on his back or sound side if a swelling and hot redness appear there.
Stop treating him and splint him until the swelling subsides, then place on him what calms it as previously mentioned. Then return to splinting and tightening it as is proper. If there are fragments or something has broken off from its extremities, do not remove them.
And do not touch him, rather mend it from the outside as we said and leave it to tighten until he recovers.
Chapter Eleven.
On Splinting a Fracture of the Arm.
The arm is what lies between the elbow and the head of the shoulder. If it has broken, splint it on both sides. One way to do this is to take a wooden rod that has been smoothed out and medium in thickness as follows:
And tie two straps on its ends, then hang it from a high place and have the sawyer sit in a chair. Then throw his broken arm onto the rod until his elbow is pressed into the middle of the rod's bend. Then hang something heavy over him.
Or have a servant hold him down below and then the doctor mend the break with his hands together until the break returns to its proper place. The other way is for the sawyer to lie on a board and hang his hand from his neck with a strap.
Then order two servants to arrange one of them above the break with his hands and the other below it and each of them extend towards its place. And if you want the extension to be stronger, tie a band beneath the break and on top of it and have each of the servants extend it to its place.
If the break is close to the armpit it should be that [middle] of the band is under the armpit and the other under the break towards the elbow. And likewise if the break is close to the elbow it should be the band on that place and on the elbow itself.
Then arrange the break gently without force until the break is even and well-settled. Then tighten it if it did not show a severe swelling, but if it showed a severe swelling then leave tightening until the seventh day and put on him a woolen pad moistened with vinegar and rose oil.
Until the swelling subsides, then tighten it. The method of tightening is to place a pad on the break, then place a sheet of new cloth on the pad, then bring the arm back onto its armpit and place your hand open on your shoulder.
And place the cloth and the tightening on the armpit and arm so that the arm serves as a substitute for the clavicles, if you are not prevented from doing so or if nothing has changed with the broken bone. But if you were to fear that something would change with it then use the clavicles.
And put on the break itself a splint that is wider and stronger than any other splints, and to make a space of one finger's width between each splint, and let the length of the splints be according to the break with an increase of three fingers from both sides.
Then tighten on the splints the tightening that I mentioned at first.
It should be tightened firmly over the place of the break.
And whatever was the tightness after the break, if you see that putting plates and tightening as we said at the time of setting of the member from your time then do it.
And if you are afraid of the hot swelling, then leave the tightness and plates until the seventh day as we said; then check the dressing every three days so that there may not be a scratch or swelling or obstruction to food reaching the member in case the tightness was excessive, it will all be cured according to what we mentioned.
If you are certain that nothing will happen, then do not remove the dressing except after many days; and the limb will be twisted on its back with its hand on its abdomen and it is placed under the arm wrapped in a soft cloth of moderate temperature.
And inspect it at all times whether at night or day so that the shape of the broken member does not become distorted or the bandage becomes loose, then correct everything with your effort; and make the food of the injured one in the order we mentioned to be gentle at first until if the bone intends to strengthen.
It is necessary that his food be thickened, for the shoulder and leg are accustomed to harden in forty days; when that happens it is necessary to remove it and he takes a bath and is treated with ointments that cure this; if the break is very deep and stable.
Do not remove the bandage from him until fifty days or until two months.
Chapter 12
In setting a fracture of the arm.
The arm is composed of two bones and are called the humeri, one of them small which is next to the fist and the other large which is below the small one from underneath; perhaps the larger bone alone was broken or the smaller one alone was broken or both were broken.
When the small upper ligament is broken, its reduction is easy and speedy. But when the lower one breaks, its fracture is difficult and its cure is slow. And if both bones are fractured with it, then take care that the bone to which the small upper ligament was attached did not break.
It is necessary for the physician to make its length short and gentle in order to set it straight. If the large ligament was broken, he should make its length stronger. And if both ligaments were broken, then he should make the length much stronger.
It is necessary to put the shape of the hand in its place when it is set, and to stretch its length on a cushion, with the index finger higher than all the other fingers. The little finger should be lower than the other fingers, and the thumb should be raised above itself.
And let there be a cushion beside it at his height so that the little finger does not suffer hardship.
Then the servant of the arm will extend it from below either by his hand or with a band, and another servant will extend it from above in the same manner. Then the physician will set the bone until he returns it to its best shape possible. If there are fragments of the bone, he will take them out.
Make every fragment return to its place with great effort. If a clean and exposed fragment appeared which was coming out through the skin, and you have no desire to set it, cut into it and pull it out in the manner we mentioned earlier. And if the fracture is with an injury, then I have opened for him a door.
You will take the treatment of it from there. And if a hot swelling appears in front of your first reduction, then cover it with a cloth made of papyrus dipped in rose oil and white wax, and let it be medium between thickness and softness, and tie it on him gently until the swelling subsides.
Therefore, remove the splints and put the prepared poultice of flour with white egg mixed together. Then take the bone plasters and let the one be placed on the break itself be a little wider and stronger; know that there are six arm splints in most cases.
The fracture was in one or both bones, then make your grasp on the place of the break stronger and tighter. The more you turn the grasp upwards or downwards, make the tightness a little looser as previously mentioned at the beginning of this chapter.
And let the bandage that is wrapped on the break be a soft and moist linen bandage, not too stiff. Let the thread that ties it from cotton of medium thickness as we have described, and tighten the limb and the bandage after some days.
If something happens that needs to be corrected like a bruise appearing on the limb, it is necessary to loosen the limb with warm water until the bruising subsides. And let the limb not be tightly bandaged for a night so that it may rest. Then tighten again.
If the tightness has relaxed and the bone has moved or something like that, then correct all of this with your effort. And also look if food is prevented from reaching the limb because of excessive tightness; it should be loosened a little and left for some days until food reaches it, then tighten it again.
If nothing is exposed to the open air from what we have mentioned, then it should not be uncovered except after twenty days or something like that. Then hang the culprit's hand around his neck; let his arm be moderate and he take care of himself from agitated movements and make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture of the arm is cured in thirty days or in two and thirty, perhaps it will be cured in twenty-eight days; all that according to the cases of temperament and the conditions of strength.
Chapter Thirteen.
In the treatment of fractures of the hand and fingers.
The palm of the hand and the joints of the fingers are rarely exposed to fracture, but frequently to inflammation, so if a break or inflammation is presented.
The operator should sit elevated and in front of him a stool at the same level.
Then he puts his hand on it stretched out, then the bone's servant is extended and straightened by the doctor until if it was united in a good union so when that happens the plaster and the splint should be lifted, unless a warm swelling occurs, then a cast is placed from above according to the place.
And I have placed it in a linen cloth, if the break is towards the bottom towards the palm of the hand then make a ball shape from the cloth and order the operator to catch on with his broken hand, then tie with a long cloth.
And let the cast be made of soft leather so that the skin will adhere with the flexion of all the hand and tie it as is necessary, if the break is to the outside then it should be made a cast from above and another cast from below in the hand so that the hand is open standing.
Then he takes the tie as the hand turns and binds it between the fingers with a bandage, if the break is to one of the joints of the finger then if the thumb, let it be straightened as necessary then tie it with the hand, and if you wanted to make for him an upright small cast to fix the break.
And let it not move, if the break is to other fingers such as the middle finger or the ring finger or the little finger then straighten and bind with the next healthy finger or bind all of them on a splint then that is better or place on top of it an upright small cast.
As we said in the case of the eyebrow, and examine after you have tied your patient's limb and removed him from the hot swelling. If something like that happens to it as often described.
Chapter Fourteen.
For tying a fracture of the thigh.
The femur often breaks and can be felt as it moves forward and backward. Its binding is that a bandage is tied above the break and another below it, and the patient lies on his back.
Then each servant will stretch their bandage to its end on an evenness, if the break is in the middle of the bone. And if it is close to the femur's joint, it should be bound with a linen band, either from wool or something like it.
When the break is near the hip to about the pubic bone so that the stretch goes upwards and the other bandage under the break. And similarly, if the break is near the knee, let the bandage be near the knee so that the stretch goes down. Then the doctor will straighten the bone with both hands until it returns to its natural shape.
The bone will be well aligned. Then it is necessary to keep the compress and bandage in place if no hot swelling occurs, but if a hot swelling does occur in the limb, leave it for some days until the swelling subsides then return to its treatment,.
As for binding it, it is necessary to wrap a hard and wide turban on the break two or three times with some of it remaining. Then bend the leg until the ankle is at the heel and insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee from below, and its ends will be above from both sides.
Then turn around the leg and thigh what remained of the turban. Then place on the thigh in the place of the break itself splints, and make one splint on the bone of the leg. Then fill the gap between the thigh and the leg with linen padding to even out the tension.
Then start by pulling firmly from the middle on the site of the break three times or four, and whenever you reapply the bandage to the site of the break let your pull be weaker and looser.
Then proceed to the ends of the thread which you had inserted between the thigh and leg, tie with what follows from above then pass through the ends to the bottom until it reaches the ankle bone, and pull them also firmly on the other side.
So that the bandage does not come undone, and then let the tension on it remain as long as the member does not become swollen or inflamed or bloated or anything like that; but if any of that happens, hasten to correct it and to treat what has happened in all that we have mentioned.
If there is in the bone a splinter that protrudes it should be straightened if you can; otherwise cut over it and remove it, and treat the wound with what we have mentioned before until it heals. And this break may be strengthened from the thigh without adding to it the leg with bandages.
As we mentioned in the arm and shoulder, except that this fracture is not exposed like them to ulceration; but if you bandage it alone without adding to it the leg then its owner will be permanently crippled.
Know that the thigh takes strength in fifty days or a little more or a little less, all of that according to the differences of temperaments and other circumstances.
Chapter Fifteenth.
In Fracture of the Patella.
The patella of the knee is rarely exposed to a fracture and the crack is often exposed to it; if it is exposed to a fracture then it will only be either split or cracked in its parts, and this may be with an open wound or without.
He must feel with his hands all of this, and mend it by bringing together the scattered parts with fingers until they come together and adhere as much as possible through gentle touch, skill and delicacy. Then he must apply a dressing and place upon it a rolled up splint if necessary.
She must bind over the appropriate tightening, then attend to all of the situations which we have described in other fractures such as the hot swelling and others by matching each obstacle with what is suitable for it until he recovers.
The 16th Chapter.
In Mending a Fracture of the Leg.
The leg has two bones, one of them is thick and is called the bone of the leg and the other is thin and is called a fibula, to which are exposed all types of fractures as those of the humerus. And because of this, its mending is like that of the humerus in all ways. If both bones break, then the leg will turn in every direction, and if the thin bone breaks it will turn forward, but if the thick bone breaks below and that becomes obvious to you.
So use the compression and alignment, and binding of splints according to that. Except that if the fracture of the leg is a severe one with many fragments, then the compression should be less and lighter, and you should strive to mend it gently.
And in the leg there is an additional thing that you do compared to the arm, which is that if you have aligned the splints and finished all of your work, take two cedar sticks or reeds or something like them.
And choose from among them what is a little thick but not from the flat, and make their length along the leg from the knee down. Then wrap each of them with two bandages wrapped around it in its length, and place one against the bone and the other on the opposite side.
So let the limb be from the knee to below the foot, then tie the two bandages in three places on both sides and in the middle; with this handle the leg will not incline to the right or left nor become cramped so well. And a splint of wood may be used along the length of the leg.
It is put in for him to preserve it from movement and more than what should be done, that in a fracture if there is a special wound. Then examine the leg every day and take care of it greatly concerning the swelling or inflation or any such thing. So when something of that happens to him then treat him with what is appropriate until he recovers. The bone of the leg heals in thirty days or near it.
Chapter Seventeen.
In fractures of the leg and fingers.
As for the ankle, it does not expose itself to fracture at all. And as for the leg bones, they may expose themselves to fracture. The fingers also rarely expose themselves to fracture and rather expose themselves to laceration in most cases.
If it is exposed to leg bones fracture and you have seen that some of them have leaned against each other, then let the surgeon put his foot on the ground upright like walking. Then go and place your foot on what has risen from those bones, and press and stretch it.
Until it returns to its places, then take the pad and splint from above and place under the sole of the foot a small board which has flat heads, then tie it with the leg firmly after wrapping it with cloth and other things that are needed.
If three or four days have passed for him, then release the bandage. You will find that the bones are levelled out whether they were broken or disjointed. So do the treatment as you see fit. And if some fingers were fractured, then treat them and stretch them as I have described to you in treating the fingers of the hand.
Then put a splint on the fractured finger, making it slightly wider than it. Then place this board under the foot which I have described to you and tie it tightly. If the bones that broke were two or three or more.
So put on each finger a splint of the same length as the cloth is folded and tie the board tightly at the bottom of the foot so that each end is protruding outside the calf.
And it is incumbent upon you not to forget what I have told you about all the symptoms that we mentioned and face each symptom with what is necessary.
The Eighteenth Chapter.
For the fracture of the female pubis and the male os penis.
When the female pubis breaks, place her on a raised surface then bend her slightly back and hold from behind. Then fill the gap between the pubis with cotton until it fills it and becomes like a ball in her vagina.
Then shake the woman and raise her pelvis slightly, until that cotton comes out so it becomes like a ball at the entrance of the vagina and the bone fracture returns. Then place a pad on her back. Whenever she wants to urinate remove the cotton gently so she can urinate.
Then return it to the way I put it first then go back to her original pad. Do this for seven days or so, and it will heal. And if you want to take a sponge, tie a tube on her mouth and enter the entire bladder into her vagina.
