Then blow into the tube with strength until the bladder swells inside the vagina, and the fracture will return. Then fill it with cotton and set her up for days as we described until she heals. And as for when the bone of the penis or the vagina breaks in a man or woman, use the same treatment in its fixing.
The repair of what we have described in the bone of the thigh, and it is not hidden from you that this fracture is rare, especially for him who had some debility. My letterbook explains this to be a good thing because the break itself guides you most of the time.
Correctly treating and binding it, so you understand, as for mentioning the man if his thigh is broken like a heron's neck then the penis enters into it, then a piece of cloth is put on top of it and bandaged and he is left for three days or so until it heals.
Chapter Nineteen.
In the treatment of fractures with a wound.
It is incumbent upon him who has a fracture with an injury, and especially if the bone is large like the bone of the thigh or arm or similar to it, that he hastens to clean out his pus as we have presented its conditions.
If the wound is bleeding with blood, it is necessary to hasten to stop its bleeding by sprinkling powdered alum on it if something else is not available then, take care of binding the fracture that very day itself and do not delay.
If it does not form a hot abscess, then do nothing. But if it does form a hot abscess, leave the bandage until the ninth day so that the abscess settles down, and do not approach him on the third or fourth day at all because he will be subject to bad symptoms.
If the fractured bone is protruding on the skin and exposed, you should try to cover it and set it with your hand gently and a short extension. And if you are not able to do so with your hand, then use this instrument, which is made of iron, its length being about seven fingers or eight. And shape it according to the size of the wound.
And for that reason, the doctor should make three or four of them according to what he needs in each type of fracture so that they are curved and slightly thick so that they do not bend when pressed upon while working.
Its tip is obtuse and its extremity has an angle, and the top of it thickens, and from its middle to the bottom it tapers off very much. This is its form:
It is called in Greek the pyramidal small peg, so its obtuse tip should be pressed onto the edge of the bone that comes out and push it with a stroke until the bone returns to some flatness, then the edges of the fracture will come together.
If the fractured tip is thin and the instrument does not take hold of it well, then cut off the end of that bone until the instrument takes hold of it. And if you are unable to return the bone as we described, then cut it off with the cutting pieces which we mentioned.
Or spread it with one of the fingers as much as you can, then clear what remains in the bone from roughness and soft rind. If after returning the bone you found the ligament to be very painful and inflamed, then know that the bone did not return to its natural place.
If you were able to return it to its natural place, then do so, for you will benefit the ligament a great deal. If you have completed your setting of the bone, then dip a cloth in black styptic wine, especially if you are in summer, and do not put any ointment on the wound.
Lest rot and corruption occur in it, then use the bandages when you are free from setting the bone and leave the wound open by making a hole with scissors in the folds to the size of the wound.
And beware, beware of pulling the wound together with the fracture, for many ignorant doctors have done so and caused their patients either death or gangrene or putridity. And let your pulling be loose contrary to pulling other fractures.
If the wound is minor or was a large one and you feared some of the bad symptoms that we mentioned, which brought discomfort to the place. Then it should not be covered with bandages but rather make him rolls of thick leather in place of bandages and bind them.
If, after a day or two, you saw the wound starting to form pus in it, then remove from him the dressing that you placed on him with drink and then apply lint and ointments which are used by us for treating wounds such as quadruple ointment and similar ones. And you should untie the bandage.
And inspect the wound every day morning and evening until it closes and heals, and you should position the limb in a way that the pus can flow easily downwards. And if many days have passed for the wound without its discharge stopping or clearing up, then know that there are small fragments of bone.
And you should search the wound with a probe, and if any of those fragments are dead then remove them and take them out, and if some of them were not dead but were infecting the limb and causing pain then cut into pieces and remove them in any way you can do that.
If a fever, or an ulcer, or another type of decay and corruption appeared over the wound, then you should face each one of them with what treatment that is suited to it as we mentioned in its section.
And what is necessary for you to be mindful of, when you are told that a large bone has broken and it has set in the limb such as the femur and the humerus and similar major limbs, then do not attempt to pull or remove it. And that often leads to death, so leave him alone until he becomes putrid, perhaps it will fall out of itself after twenty or thirty days.
So treat the wound if you see it in need of treatment, otherwise leave it alone.
Chapter Twenty.
In the treatment of complicated wounds that result from some fractures.
This complication often occurs after the fracture is reduced, especially near the joints, making the limb look ugly and may prevent it from performing its natural function.
Observe, if the complication is soft then use medicines such as senna, myrrh, camphor, and gentian, either some of them or all of them, mixed with a binding draught or egg whites or vinegar.
And apply it to the complication in a compress and tightly bind it with good pressure, and leave the bandage on for many days until it is resolved, then repeat the treatment again until the complication goes away if God wills. Or tie a well-made lead plate over it.
If the lead plate has a special property that will get rid of everything inside the members, and if the complication has hardened and become worse and necessity demands its removal, then make an incision above it and cut out or remove the dead tissue with some forceps until it is gone and treat the wound until it heals.
Chapter Twenty-One.
In the treatment of fractures if they have become stiff and the limb has remained weak after that unlike its natural state.
If a fracture has become stiffened and the limb has remained thereafter weak, it is only due to many reasons. One of them is either that the bandages have been wrapped too tightly or tied in a wrong manner, or the bandages have been tightened excessively so as to prevent nourishment from reaching the limb, or excessive massage has been applied repeatedly, or the limb has been moved excessively at an inappropriate time, or there has been a lack of blood and weakness in the body of the patient.
To treat this, feed the patient and nourish his body until there is an abundance of blood in him. Then, use a warm bath on him and induce joy and happiness upon him and suchlike. After that, place a poultice on the limb so that it attracts abundant food to it or cools it down with lukewarm water. So that nourishment may enter into him and he may return to his natural shape.
Chapter 22.
In the treatment of a fractured bone if it has become deformed and prevented from functioning as it should.
When a limb, having been fractured, has become deformed or bent due to the bone being disarranged or complicated and as a result its shape is distorted. Unless the limb was not prevented from functioning naturally, it is not fitting for one to claim that it should be broken again. And indeed many ignorant physicians and practitioners have done so in our land.
And this deed is very blameworthy and leads to great harm, but if the deformation and complication are soft, then one should soak it in water that has been boiled with marshmallow roots such as al-hasmah, its stem and its crown and the like.
And it is plastered with boiled poultices such as well-made diakalonia or taken from the saliva of a peacock and beaten together with chicken fat and bull's ghee, or taken from the juice of a ripe plum and pounded with pigeon dung and the like among the medications that are called breaker of adhesions.
And it may be that the complication will break down with a constant and fine oil made from almonds, and the limb should be moved in all directions at all times. But if the deformation has become pronounced and hardened and necessity demands its treatment with iron.
It is necessary that you should cut above it and detach the bone connection, and cut away any remaining complication or bone with fine sections, using a bandage for this with effort and care; then treat the wound as has been mentioned before until it heals.
The Twenty-Third Chapter.
The jaw is an exit of a joint from its place, preventing it from movement and uglifying the shape of the member, and causing severe pains and aches. If one of the jaws is presented, it should be quickly returned to its place.
He should not be delayed at all, for if he is delayed the area will become inflamed and it will become difficult to return the jaw. For this reason, he should not delay nor move him nor stretch in the time that the jaw becomes inflamed, for painful swelling and annoying pains often occur.
But if that occurs, he should quickly proceed to lance the swelling and then leave it until the inflammation subsides a little, then he shall massage the member with hot water and oil, then return it gently and treat each member as has been mentioned before in its place.
And the chapters on the jaw were also arranged according to what was mentioned before in the fracture, from top to bottom.
The Twenty-Fourth Chapter.
Concerning the treatment of the lower jawbone.
The jawbones are rarely dislodged, but when they are it is either a slight displacement where the bones move out of their place slightly and become somewhat loose, or a complete dislocation where the bones completely come out of their place until they become very loose. It extends as far as the chest so that saliva flows out of the swelling, and he is unable to hold it, nor can he bear to bite with his jaw and his tongue trembles in speech. But if the dislocation is slight, it usually returns by itself easily.
As for the removal of a fully intact tooth, it is necessary to hasten its return and not postpone it at all. A servant should grasp the head of the patient, while the physician inserts the tip of one hand into the root of the jaw inside his mouth if the jawbone is on one side.
Or he inserts the tips of both hands into it if the jawbone is on both sides, while all his fingers are outside guiding them. He orders the patient to loosen and let go of his jaw so that the physician can straighten out the jaw and push it back into its place.
If it is difficult to return them, especially if both jawbones are involved, then use the medicinal paste with hot water and oil until it becomes easy to return them. Do not postpone returning them as we have said. If they have returned and been straightened out, and the patient's mouth has closed and not stretched, then put on them a poultice of linen cloth made from beeswax and fat and safflower oil.
Then tie him gently with a relaxed bandage. The patient should lie on his back with his head supported between two pillows so that he does not move it to the right or left, nor attempt to chew anything. But make his food mushy and smooth.
Until the pain has gone and the jawbone has closed, let him eat what seems good to him. And have him use that gently and not force his mouth open when eating, drinking, or yawning until the jawbone has closed and been healed.
If it is difficult to return the jawbones if they have been dislodged at some point and not returned to their place, then often from this happens fevers and permanent headaches, and perhaps the patient's belly will be loose or he will vomit repeatedly. If you see that, know that it has failed.
And often death occurs to him as a result of this within ten days.
Chapter Twenty-Five.
For the reduction of a broken scapula and a fractured shoulder blade.
But the scapula does not break inwards because of its connection to the chest, and it will break outwards which can be felt by touch. Its reduction is for the patient to lie on their back and stretch their arms.
Then you press the area with your fist with great pressure, and it will return, then place on it a compress and a bandage and tie it. And as for its angle which follows the shoulder blade and is connected to it, it hardly breaks off except in rarity. If it does break off one day, it should be returned and straightened as we have mentioned and what you prepare for.
Then you place on it a compress and a bandage and tie, and order the patient to bind themselves with tightness and quiet until they heal. And by this treatment alone will the angle of the shoulder blade return if it also has moved from its place.
Chapter Twenty-Six.
For the reduction of a fractured humerus.
Know that the humerus breaks in three ways, one is that it breaks towards the armpit downwards and the second is that it breaks towards the chest and perhaps it will break above the elbow, which is rare.
It does not break towards the back from the place of the shoulder and it also does not break forward from the place of the nerve, but rather it is more likely to break and come out downwards towards the armpit, especially in those with thin limbs because it comes out quickly in them and goes back in quickly.
And as for those with thick limbs, it is the opposite, meaning that it comes out slowly and goes back in slowly. And perhaps a blow or a fall will strike some people so that the humerus becomes swollen and hot, and they think it has broken, so it should be checked until its safety is confirmed.
When the fracture is thus approached, and the jawbone can be felt going down towards the armpit. If you compare the splintered shoulder blade with a healthy one, you will find a clear difference between them. You will feel the top of the shoulder blade in the splintered one to be rough, and under the armpit when touched, the head of the shoulder blade is like an egg.
The healer is unable to raise his hand to his ear and move it in all motions, and similarly if the fracture has occurred towards the chest or upwards then you will find that clear at touch which does not hide.
And this jawbone may be easy to return if it was soft or the healer is a youth, and its return that you have a servant raise his hand up then place your thumbs under his armpit and lift the joint with force up to its place and the servant raises his hand and extends it up.
Then he should place it down below and it will return quickly, if it did not return as we mentioned and the jawbone has been in that condition for several days then the healer should wash himself in hot water and use the senna which relaxes and becomes soft like cooked tongue root and mallow and the crown of the king in the water.
Then the healer should lie on his back and put a ball of wool under his armpit that is moderate between softness and firmness then the doctor should place his elbow on the ball and take hold of the patient's hand with his own and extend it down.
And he pushes the ball with his leg then it returns to its place, and if you wish to return it in this way that you should have a man taller than the patient take hold of him from the side and enter his shoulder blade under the patient's armpit and raise his armpit up until the patient is suspended in the air. And another servant pulls the patient's hand down to his stomach then if the patient is light it is necessary that something else should be hung on him so that he may carry him, then the jawbone returns to its place, and it can also return in another way.
It is to concentrate in the earth a long beam whose head is rounded like a mortar, not too thick nor too thin; then it is placed under the patient's armpit after having put on top of the beam a piece of linen and the operator standing on top of the beam.
Then she extends her hand to the other side and stretches her body also from the other side with force; then the joint returns to its place quickly. If difficulty of its return occurs due to all that was mentioned, use this treatment, which is to take a beam whose length is about two arms. And expose it as much as four fingers and thicken it like two fingers; it should have a rounded head so that it is easy to enter the depth of the armpit in this way.
Then it is tied on the rounded head a piece of linen so that the beam does not hurt him. Then he makes it stay under the armpit and stretches his hand, arm, and wrist all together onto the beam downwards; the beam is tied on the shoulder, elbow, and hand.
Then the arm is placed on a support. The support is raised and the hand is stretched downwards; the rest of the body is left hanging from the other side. Then the joint enters through its socket, so if it enters in any way possible, it should be placed under the armpit a ball made of wool of moderate size.
Then the prepared poultice is carried with gum, resin, and white of egg on the shoulder in all its parts as it turns from above. Then the ball is pulled strongly under the armpit and held firmly; his hand is tied to his neck.
He is left without moving his hand for seven days. It should be that the operator makes the patient's food a little less so that the part becomes stronger, and it is faster to heal. Then after seven or five days he is exercised with movement; if it has stood firm and not stiffened, then he has been cured.
If the joint is repeatedly dislocated due to moisture exposed to it or another defect, he should apply Kii with three safflower oils as mentioned in the chapter on Kii. If he has done this and loosened the bandage after seven days and the joint has not remained fixed.
He has reapplied the poultice and tightened it again several times, but it still did not remain fixed, and fell down, relaxed, and could not be lifted above. Then know that its nerve in the head of the humerus has been severed or stretched out or relaxed. Then know that this joint will never remain firm in its place ever.
And as for the jaw which is towards the chest and breast, and if it is displaced to the back, it should be pushed back with the hands until it returns. Other treatments and remedies should be used in it until it heals. If an ulceration appears on the part of the body after healing and it moves slowly.
So let the doctor use hot water several times until that ulceration softens and returns to its original state.
The Twenty-Seventh Section.
In the treatment of elbow joint dislocation.
The elbow joint dislocates easily and also returns easily, and it is dislocated to all sides, especially to the front or to the back, and its dislocation will not be hidden from you because it is visible to sight and touch in any shape it becomes dislocated.
And if you compare the dislocated elbow with the normal one, it will be clear to you by a clear indication that the joint has sunk and cannot bend the arm nor can you touch it with your humerus, and you must hasten and return the dislocation in your time before applying warm poultice.
For it is if he applies a warm poultice to it, difficulty returns it and perhaps will not be cured at all, especially if the dislocation is to the back, for that is the most difficult and painful of all types of dislocations and death often accompanies it.
He should be placed on his back if it is possible for a servant to extend his hands and arms wide, the doctor's hand from above the elbow and under it, and he pushes the joint with both thumbs or the base of his hand until it returns to its place.
As for the jaw that is forward, it should be returned by bending the hand once so as to strike with the knuckled base of the wrist which is adjacent. If this does not cause the jaw to return, a very strong push should be made and two servants should extend their arms.
The patient should also be held by two servants so that he does not move when pushed. Then the arm is turned in every direction after being wrapped with a folded cloth or wide bandage, and if the physician starts to treat the joint with his hands, he rubs them with oil so that it may aid in the ease of sliding the joint.
Then the joint is pushed with a strong push until it returns. And after its return, a poultice of dough that has been kneaded and dried should be placed on it, tied tightly and securely, and the arm is held from the neck of the patient for some days.
Then wait, if the joint remains in its place then remove the bandage from it and leave it. And if you see that the joint does not strengthen, but rather deteriorates after returning to its place and being removed, then repeat the poultice and bandage and leave it for some days until it strengthens, then remove it.
And if it becomes difficult to move, he should be bathed in a warm bath with gentle soap and friction until it loosens or an oil-soaked cloth of a fat goat is placed on the joint. Then tie it and leave it on for a day and night then remove it and put him in the bath. And when he sweats, rub the joint with a moderate friction.
Then repeat the operation on him once, twice, and thrice with his entering the bath until it relaxes; if you wish to make tallow of ox fat moist and soft upon him do so several times until it relaxes and returns to its original state.
Chapter Twenty-Eight.
For the treatment of a broken collarbone.
The collarbone of the hand often breaks and its fracture is easy to cure compared to other joints, except that it should be hastened to set the collarbone the moment it breaks before a swelling or inflammation occurs in the place.
To set the collarbone, place the broken collarbone on a board and have an assistant stretch out his hand and place the doctor's palm on the navel of the joint and push until it returns; except that one should look if the collarbone has gone to the inside of the arm.
Let the patient place his hand's palm on the board when pushing and pulling, and if the collarbone is prominent to the outside of the arm, then let him put the inside of his hand on the board so that the doctor's hand falls on the same navel of the joint; if it returns immediately, otherwise hold it with a soothing plaster for swelling.
And leave it so, as it cannot be put back once the days have passed; except that the joint will remain in its place and the patient will not be harmed anything, unless the hand becomes stiff and unable to grasp anything.
So then know that the nerve has been severed or crushed, and there is no remedy for it except with a binding compress; maybe it will benefit and maybe it will not. If the collarbone is set, then place on it the plaster which we have described, bind and leave for five days.
Then it will heal and the hand will be exercised; if its movement is stiffened and there is something like a swelling in it, then soften it with warm water and linseed oil several times until it relaxes.
The 29th chapter.
For the treatment of finger paralysis.
The fingers may paralyse in every direction, so if a finger paralyses towards the front of the palm or its back, stretch out the finger and push the paralysis with your thumb until it returns; then bind the head of the finger and suspend it toward the side to which it had paralysed.
Leave it for two days then release it and stretch it until it becomes straight for a day, if it be at night tie it up the same way, so it will remain in place by day and be exercised by movement and tied up at night do that for many days until it is strengthened.
And do the same with it if it paralyses towards the palm of the hand, tie it towards the same side and do to it what you did first until it recovers, and also do the same with it whenever it paralyses in any other direction.
The 30th chapter.
For the treatment of paralysis of the loin vertebrae.
When paralysis occurs to a vertebra from the vertebrae of the back or the neck, complete paralysis or if many vertebrae are separated from their places there is no treatment for it because death rushes towards the injured.
And a sign of that is that the patient's stool comes out without his will and he cannot hold it, and often some of his limbs stiffen either his legs or his arms or one of them; and if one vertebra separates from its place then many separate also.
And its separation can be to four sides, the one that separates towards the back is called a hadda, and as for its treatment, look if the hadda occurred from childhood then there is no treatment in it nor recovery at all, but if it happened due to a fall or blow and so on.
The ancients have mentioned it in various treatments with lengthy words, most of which are not beneficial. I have simplified what they have said, drawing near to the meaning and explaining it, making the instrument different from what they showed and described.
I say, the abscess that occurs in front of the chest, there is no remedy for it nor cure, and similarly those that occur on both sides. But only the one that occurs in the back, particularly what I have described, is treated by having the patient lean forward.
On a low bench next to a wall, he is placed with a wet mat beneath him so that his chest will not be harmed. Then, one beam standing upright in a pit in the ground near his head, and another beam near his feet on the other side of the bench.
And a servant holds the beam, so that it is not firmly in the pit, and another servant holds the other one in this manner. Then, he is wrapped on his chest and under his arms with a soft and tight cloth bandage, extending the end of the bandage to the beam near his head and tying him to it.
Then, another bandage is pulled over his thighs and shins and near his knee joints. Then, all the bandages are gathered together and tied to the other beam that is at his feet. Then, each servant pulls the beam towards their end with the bandage and they do not remove the beams from their original position.
Unless they are not firmly in place as I said, the physician places his hands on the axle with force until it returns, or he puts a board on it then leans on the board with his feet until it returns. If it does not return with this treatment, he takes a board that is three arms' length long.
A hole is dug in the wall near the patient, a place where the end of the board will enter. Then, the middle part of the board is placed on the abscess and the physician places his feet on the other end and tightens tightly until the axle is compressed.
When it returns to its place, and if you wish to make it with the ring that is made by hand which is that you pierce into the earth at the head of the ulcer in the end of the splint two wooden sticks whose length is an arm's length and at his feet also two wooden sticks.
Let it be that after every stick a span is left and that in each stick a hole is made through which the ring passes and all the wood is tied to the earth so that it does not move at all, and a rod is inserted turned over, which is the ring in which the bandage winds around the two wooden sticks.
And at its end a hole is made which has been tied with a rod the length of a span to turn it around, and similarly in the other two wooden sticks, then pull the bandage that you tightened over his chest into the ring that is near his head, and the bandages that you tightened on his legs into the ring that is near his feet.
Then a servant will stand at each ring and hold the stick with which the ring is turned, while the physician will adjust the splint as we have mentioned. And this is the picture of the ring and the splints and the ulcer:.
Then, after the splints have been returned and the place has been straightened, it is necessary to carry the dried poultice with white of egg and then linen cloth, and then put a wooden board over the poultice as a cast which will be three fingers wide or so, and its length will take the place of the swelling.
And on some of the healthy stones, then tie with the bandage that should be used and the ulcer will eat gentle food until it is healed, if there remains some swelling in the place at the end of the healing, it is necessary to use the medicines that are applied and softened along with the wooden board as we have described for a long time.
And the plate of lead can be used for that, and there may remain a swelling at the end of the ribs which will be thought to be an inflammation but it is an excess bone which has become swollen so do not expose it to this treatment lest death result.
The 30th chapter.
For the treatment of the broken hip.
Know that the hip joint and the glenohumeral joint are only exposed to fracture, not to mild dislocation or subluxation, and the hip joint fractures on four sides.
For it fractures into the joint, outside, forward and backward, and more often does so into the joint, and a sign that it has fractured into the joint is that if you compare the healthy leg of the patient with the afflicted one, the afflicted will be shorter.
And the knee will be flexed more than normal, and the patient will not be able to bend his leg when he lies down, and the area that follows the flexion is warm and swollen even before the head of the femur has reached there.
And a sign that it has fractured outside is that its symptoms are opposite to these, and a sign that it has fractured forward is that he will straighten his leg completely except that he cannot bend it without pain; if he tries to walk forward, he will not be able to do so.
And he will have difficulty urinating and his leg will be rigid; when he walks, his heel will strike the ground, and a sign that it has fractured backward is that the patient will not be able to straighten the knee, nor can he bend it before the ankle bends, and his leg will also be shorter than the other.
And the ankle is relaxed and the head of the femur is at the place of the patella, as clear; but the treatment of this fracture is that you should examine, if the fracture has been old and worn out with its owner and he did not try to reduce it and remained in its state then there is no treatment for it whatsoever.
So you should not expose it to this, and as for him whose fracture is recent, whatever its type was from the four sides of the fracture, hasten to flex the joint and insert it into the inside and outside and move it with the right and left hands so perhaps.
Return and do not resort to any other treatment. If he did not return in this way, it is advisable that a servant with strong hands should carry him on his back from below, either by means of his hands or else by a litter fastened to his leg above the knee, another servant carrying him from above.
That he should insert his hands under his armpits, then bind him with a soft bandage on the pubic bone and that another servant should take hold of one end of the bandage and another servant carry him either from in front between the armpit and shoulder blade to the lower back or from behind towards the back.
And all of them should carry him at once until the patient is lifted by his body from the ground and remains suspended. If the jaw returned as we mentioned, otherwise it is necessary for each type to do what I mentioned in special treatment.
But the specific treatment if the jaw returned inside, it is necessary for the patient to lie on his correct side then bind a bandage around the pubic bone between the head of the femur and the place under the armpit.
Then the bandage should be extended upwards from the armpit to the top of the body towards the shoulder blade, and another strong servant should take hold with his arms and grasp the thick part from the upper thigh and extend it outward with a firm pull that he returns to its place.
And this type is easier than the rest of the treatment types that can return this member, if it was difficult for you and did not respond to entering with this type of treatment at all, then bind the patient's legs together with a strong soft bandage on the ankles and knees.
And it will be after each of them as much as four fingers, and the upper leg will be extended more than the other by two fingers, then hang the patient on a beam in the house so that he is away from the ground by two arms.
Then order a strong slave to embrace the hip and attach another boy to it, and push the other boy holding the hip with force; then the joint will return to its place quickly. And as for his own, if it is dislocated outward.
Then the patient should lie on the splints as we have described for a person with a fractured femur, and pull tight the bandage on his upper leg and chest. Then place the two wooden splints one at each foot and the other at his head, then place an additional splint in the middle of the splints firmly tied.
He may wrap a moist bandage on it so that the patient is not harmed, and so that the splint may be between his thighs, lest he slide down when leaning forward. Then each servant will stretch towards him and the doctor will straighten the hip with his hands, if it has returned to its place.
Unless it has returned to its place, then put a board on him and fasten it as we have described for the fractured femur, unless the patient is lying on his healthy side. And if the dislocation is forward, then extend the leg of the patient once while he is in this position himself over the splints.
The doctor puts the back of his right hand on the upper part of the thigh and presses it with his other hand, yet the pressure extends downward to the knee level. And if the dislocation is behind, then he should not extend the leg downward.
And it is raised above the ground, but it should be placed on a hard thing. Also, he should not move his foot outward when it is dislocated to the outside, as we have described for him lying on the splints with his face down and the bandages tight.
As we have said, it is also necessary to apply the splint with a board on the place where the joint has dislocated. And if the hip joint has returned as it should be and its return is evident, then extend both legs of the patient.
If you see them both placed in their proper position, and the man can bend and straighten the leg without difficulty, then know that it has been reduced correctly. So join the thighs together, carry the pad and tighten it firmly, but do not let the hip move to one side or the other.
The patient is to remain at rest for three days or four. Then release the bandage and pad, and place the leg in a splint with the other one. If you see them equal in length, then know that the fracture has been set correctly; so allow the patient to walk. If you see something relaxed in it,
So return and re-bandage it, tighten it as you tightened the first time, and leave him at rest for three days. Then release it and he will gradually walk on them for a few days until he is completely healed, God willing.
The Second and Third Sections.
In the Treatment of Fracture of the Knee.
The knee-joint fractures in three ways, inward, outward and downward (i.e. to the back) but it does not fracture forward. And a sign of its fracture is that you order the operator to bind his leg into his thigh.
If it does not adhere to the thigh, know that the knee-joint is fractured. Treat all three types of fractures by having the patient sit on his heels if he is strong enough for this, and have a servant sit behind him to hold his waist and bend him slightly back; then sit you yourself on his thighs.
And lean your back against his face, and place his leg between yours; then hold his knee with both hands, interlock your fingers on his knee, and press hard with your palms on either side of his knee. And have another servant extend his leg until the knee returns to its proper position.
And a sign that it has returned to its proper position is that the leg adheres to the thigh in a soft and relaxed manner. Then bind them both together with a bandage, for three days or four; then release them. And he should not use it except for light walking until it is strong.
If it becomes difficult for you to return it to what we have described, then use the strong pressure with the straps that I have previously described in the treatment of the hip until it returns.
The Thirty-third Chapter.
In the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
The ankle may dislocate slightly, or it may become completely dislocated. Its dislocation will be either inward or outward. A sign of its dislocation is that the ankle becomes swollen and prominent in the direction to which it has dislocated.
As for the treatment of its slight dislocation, it is easy to return it. This is done by extending it gently with the hands and straightening until it returns. As for treating its complete dislocation, the patient should sit down and a strong servant should grasp him from behind, holding him in his middle.
Then grasp the patient's leg with your right hand from above and your left hand from below, at the level of the ankle. Then pull the leg towards you with your right hand, then with your left hand twice, then straighten it with your left hand and push the foot's chest with your right hand gently towards the leg without force.
Do that twice as we have described, then push the foot's chest towards the leg in the third attempt while pulling the ankle. If it returns to its normal position within one or two attempts and you see the foot evened out, otherwise repeat the treatment on it, for it will return.
If it becomes difficult for you to return it to what we have described, then lay the patient on his back on the ground and tie him down firmly to the ground with a rope that has been tied securely so that he is between his thighs. A cloth should be wrapped around him so that he does not cause harm to the patient, then the servant will tie his legs.
Then another servant should hold the patient's leg either by his hands or with a strap tied around the back of his knee. Then each servant should pull in opposition to the other, and the support should be upright between the patient's thighs so that it holds him and he does not lean forward when being pulled, then the doctor should straighten the ankle with his hands.
Another servant should hold the healthy leg down. If the jaw returns quickly, then when you see that it has returned safely, carry the splint and traction, and bind it with tight ligatures, and tie the foot with a ligature down. It is necessary to be careful of the nerve which is above the ankle from behind.
Lest the ligature be too tight and hurt him, then let it rest for two or three days. If the ligature has relaxed, then tighten it, then release it in the third or fourth day. The patient will abstain from walking for forty days.
If he tries to walk before this period, I have not guaranteed that the jaw will not break and be ruined, and then will not accept treatment afterwards. If an inflammation appears in him, it is necessary to use what we have mentioned earlier for its relief, as well as poultice until it goes away.
The Thirty-Fourth Chapter.
For the treatment of a broken jaw of the foot's fingers.
It is necessary to arrange what was separated from it with a short extension without force, and this is not difficult but easy. If the jaw is in some toes of the foot, then it is necessary for the patient to place his foot on a flat place on the ground.
Or on a board and he is standing like walking, then you go and place your foot on what has come out of those joints, then press it with your foot strongly until it returns and you see that it has settled down, and there is no nodule in the place.
Then place under the sole of his foot a board which will take all his foot and have two heads, then pull it strongly, tightly, firmly for three days, then remove it and keep him away from walking for many days until it strengthens and I am safe that it will return if God wills.
The Thirty-Fifth Chapter.
In types of jaw which are with wound or with breakage or with both together.
When something like that occurs and the treatment of it and its cure is delayed, very often death follows.
Therefore, no one should take on the treatment of something like that unless he is skilled in the craft for a long time, delicate, compassionate, and cautious, without being reckless or presumptuous, and only using in the beginning medicines that calm down hot ulcers and then surrendering to the illness.
Praise be to God, however, that I had hoped for safety from the affliction with mild sickness and you saw some hope in it, so return him to your time at first before the hot ulcer forms.
If the part returns to what you desired, then use the treatment that calms hot ulcers and treat the wound with a salve that dries quickly. If the jaw is with a fracture and there are splinters in the bone, remove them. And follow in that what we have mentioned about simple illnesses as it comes up in its places.
And strive with your best effort and free yourself from the path of arrogance as you have preceded in my advice to you, for that is safer for your dignity and more pleasing to God if He wills.
